<p>The <b>history of literature</b> is the historical development of <a href="/wiki/Writing" title="Writing">writings</a> in <a href="/wiki/Prose" title="Prose">prose</a> or <a href="/wiki/Poetry" title="Poetry">poetry</a> that attempt to provide <a href="/wiki/Entertainment" title="Entertainment">entertainment</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Enlightenment_(concept)" title="Enlightenment (concept)">enlightenment</a>, or <a href="/wiki/Education" title="Education">instruction</a> to the reader/listener/observer, as well as the development of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Literary_technique" title="Literary technique">literary techniques</a> used in the <a href="/wiki/Communication" title="Communication">communication</a> of these pieces. Not all writings constitute <a href="/wiki/Literature" title="Literature">literature</a>. Some recorded materials, such as compilations of <a href="/wiki/Data" title="Data">data</a> (e.g., a <a href="/wiki/Check_register" title="Check register">check register</a>) are not considered literature, and this article relates only to the evolution of the works defined above.
</p>
<div aria-labelledby="mw-toc-heading" class="toc" id="toc" role="navigation"><input class="toctogglecheckbox" id="toctogglecheckbox" role="button" style="display:none" type="checkbox"/><div class="toctitle" dir="ltr" lang="en"><h2 id="mw-toc-heading">Contents</h2><span class="toctogglespan"><label class="toctogglelabel" for="toctogglecheckbox"></label></span></div>

<ul>

<ul>

<h2><span id="Ancient_.28Bronze_Age.E2.80.935th_century.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Ancient_(Bronze_Age–5th_century)">Ancient (Bronze Age–5th century)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_literature&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Ancient (Bronze Age–5th century)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Ancient_literature" title="Ancient literature">Ancient literature</a></div><p>Early literature is derived from stories told in <a href="/wiki/Hunter-gatherer" title="Hunter-gatherer">hunter-gatherer</a> bands through <a href="/wiki/Oral_tradition" title="Oral tradition">oral tradition</a>, including <a href="/wiki/Myth" title="Myth">myth</a> and <a href="/wiki/Folklore" title="Folklore">folklore</a>. <a href="/wiki/Storytelling" title="Storytelling">Storytelling</a> emerged as the human mind evolved to apply <a href="/wiki/Causal_reasoning" title="Causal reasoning">causal reasoning</a> and structure events into a <a href="/wiki/Narrative" title="Narrative">narrative</a> and <a href="/wiki/Language" title="Language">language</a> allowed early humans to share information with one another. Early storytelling provided opportunity to learn about dangers and <a href="/wiki/Social_norm" title="Social norm">social norms</a> while also entertaining listeners.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1"><a href="#cite_note-1">[1]</a></sup> Myth can be expanded to include all use of patterns and stories to make sense of the world, and it may be psychologically intrinsic to humans. <a href="/wiki/Epic_poetry" title="Epic poetry">Epic poetry</a> is recognized as the pinnacle of ancient literature. These works are long narrative poems that recount the feats of mythic heroes, often said to take place in the nation's early history.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESutherland20087–16_2-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESutherland20087–16-2">[2]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/History_of_writing" title="History of writing">history of writing</a> began independently in different parts of the world, including in <a href="/wiki/Mesopotamia" title="Mesopotamia">Mesopotamia</a> about 3200 BC, in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ancient_China" title="Ancient China">Ancient China</a> about 1250 BC, and in <a href="/wiki/Mesoamerica" title="Mesoamerica">Mesoamerica</a> about 650 BC. Literature was not initially incorporated in writing, as it was primarily used for simpler purposes, such as <a href="/wiki/Accounting" title="Accounting">accounting</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3"><a href="#cite_note-3">[3]</a></sup> Some of the earliest surviving works of literature include <i><a href="/wiki/The_Maxims_of_Ptahhotep" title="The Maxims of Ptahhotep">The Maxims of Ptahhotep</a></i> and the <i><a href="/wiki/Story_of_Wenamun" title="Story of Wenamun">Story of Wenamun</a></i> from <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Egypt" title="Ancient Egypt">Ancient Egypt</a>, <i><a href="/wiki/Instructions_of_Shuruppak" title="Instructions of Shuruppak">Instructions of Shuruppak</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Poor_Man_of_Nippur" title="Poor Man of Nippur">Poor Man of Nippur</a></i> from Mesopotamia, and <i><a href="/wiki/Classic_of_Poetry" title="Classic of Poetry">Classic of Poetry</a></i> from Ancient China.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4"><a href="#cite_note-4">[4]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Mesopotamia">Mesopotamia</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_literature&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Mesopotamia">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:GilgameshTablet.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="842" data-file-width="750" decoding="async" height="247" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/26/GilgameshTablet.jpg/220px-GilgameshTablet.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/26/GilgameshTablet.jpg/330px-GilgameshTablet.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/26/GilgameshTablet.jpg/440px-GilgameshTablet.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:GilgameshTablet.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A stone tablet containing part of the <a href="/wiki/Epic_of_Gilgamesh" title="Epic of Gilgamesh">Epic of Gilgamesh</a></div></div></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Sumerian_literature" title="Sumerian literature">Sumerian literature</a> is the oldest known literature, written in <a href="/wiki/Sumer" title="Sumer">Sumer</a>. Types of literature were not clearly defined, and all Sumerian literature incorporated poetic aspects. Sumerian poems demonstrate basic elements of poetry, including <a href="/wiki/Line_(poetry)" title="Line (poetry)">lines</a>, <a href="/wiki/Imagery" title="Imagery">imagery</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Metaphor" title="Metaphor">metaphor</a>. Humans, gods, talking animals, and inanimate objects were all incorporated as characters. Suspense and humor were both incorporated into Sumerian stories. These stories were primarily shared orally, though they were also recorded by <a href="/wiki/Scribe" title="Scribe">scribes</a>. Some works were associated with specific <a href="/wiki/Musical_instrument" title="Musical instrument">musical instruments</a> or contexts and may have been performed in specific settings. Sumerian literature did not use <a href="/wiki/Title_(publishing)" title="Title (publishing)">titles</a>, instead being referred to by the work's first line.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5"><a href="#cite_note-5">[5]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Akkadian_literature" title="Akkadian literature">Akkadian literature</a> developed in subsequent Mesopotamian societies, such as <a href="/wiki/Babylonia" title="Babylonia">Babylonia</a> and <a href="/wiki/Assyria" title="Assyria">Assyria</a>, from the third to first millennia BC. During this time, it spread to other areas, including Egypt, <a href="/wiki/Ugarit" title="Ugarit">Ugarit</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Hattusa" title="Hattusa">Hattusa</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Akkadian_language" title="Akkadian language">Akkadian language</a> was influenced by the <a href="/wiki/Sumerian_language" title="Sumerian language">Sumerian language</a>, and many elements of Sumerian literature were adopted in Akkadian literature. Many works of Akkadian literature were commissioned by kings that had scribes and scholars in their service. Some of these works served to celebrate the king or the divine, while others recorded information for religious practices or medicine. Poetry, proverbs, folktales, love lyrics, and accounts of disputes were all incorporated into Akkadian literature.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Ancient_Egypt">Ancient Egypt</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_literature&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Ancient Egypt">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Egyptian_literature" title="Ancient Egyptian literature">Ancient Egyptian literature</a></div>
<p>Literature of the <a href="/wiki/Old_Kingdom_of_Egypt" title="Old Kingdom of Egypt">Old Kingdom of Egypt</a> developed directly from practical use during the <a href="/wiki/Fifth_Dynasty_of_Egypt" title="Fifth Dynasty of Egypt">Fifth Dynasty</a>. Lists of offerings to the gods were rewritten as prayers, and statistical information about state officials was expanded into <a href="/wiki/Autobiography" title="Autobiography">autobiographies</a>. These autobiographies were written to exemplify the virtues of their subjects and often incorporated a free flow style that blended prose and poetry. Kings were not written about beyond clerical recordings, but poetry was performed during the funerals of kings as part of a religious ritual. The <i>Instructions</i>, a form of <a href="/wiki/Wisdom_literature" title="Wisdom literature">wisdom literature</a> that was popular during most of Ancient Egyptian history, taught maxims of <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Egyptian_philosophy" title="Ancient Egyptian philosophy">Ancient Egyptian philosophy</a> that combined pragmatic thought and religious speculation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_7-0"><a href="#cite_note-:0-7">[7]</a></sup>
</p><p>These literary traditions continued to develop in the <a href="/wiki/Middle_Kingdom_of_Egypt" title="Middle Kingdom of Egypt">Middle Kingdom of Egypt</a> as autobiographies became more intricate. The role of the king in literature expanded during this period; royal testaments were written from the perspective of the king to his successor, and celebrations of the king and advocacy of strong leadership were included in autobiographies and <i>Instructions</i>. <a href="/wiki/Fiction" title="Fiction">Fiction</a> and analysis of <a href="/wiki/Good_and_evil" title="Good and evil">good and evil</a> also developed during this period.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_7-1"><a href="#cite_note-:0-7">[7]</a></sup> During the <a href="/wiki/New_Kingdom_of_Egypt" title="New Kingdom of Egypt">New Kingdom of Egypt</a>, the popularity of wisdom literature and educational works persisted, though the use of teachings and stories was prioritized over the use of discourses. Entertainment literature was popular among the nobility during this period, incorporating aspects of narrative myth and folklore, religious hymns, love songs, and praise for the king and the city.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Ancient_China">Ancient China</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_literature&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Ancient China">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Chinese_classics" title="Chinese classics">Chinese classics</a> and <a href="/wiki/Classical_Chinese_poetry" title="Classical Chinese poetry">Classical Chinese poetry</a></div>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Zhou_dynasty">Zhou dynasty</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_literature&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Zhou dynasty">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Rongo_Analects_02.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="540" data-file-width="712" decoding="async" height="167" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/84/Rongo_Analects_02.jpg/220px-Rongo_Analects_02.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/84/Rongo_Analects_02.jpg/330px-Rongo_Analects_02.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/84/Rongo_Analects_02.jpg/440px-Rongo_Analects_02.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Rongo_Analects_02.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <i><a href="/wiki/Analects" title="Analects">Analects</a></i> of Confucius</div></div></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Chinese_mythology" title="Chinese mythology">Chinese mythology</a> played a notable role in the earliest Chinese literature, though it was less prominent compared to mythological literature in other civilizations. By the time of the <a href="/wiki/Zhou_dynasty" title="Zhou dynasty">Zhou dynasty</a>, Chinese culture emphasized the community over the individual, discouraging mythological stories of great personages and characterization of the divine. Mythological literature was more common in the southern <a href="/wiki/Chu_(state)" title="Chu (state)">Chu</a> nation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELuo20117_9-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELuo20117-9">[9]</a></sup> The <i><a href="/wiki/Tao_Te_Ching" title="Tao Te Ching">Tao Te Ching</a></i> and the <a href="/wiki/Zhuangzi_(book)" title="Zhuangzi (book)"><i>Zhuangzi</i></a> are philosophical compilations that serve as the foundation of Taoism.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELuo201162–63_10-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELuo201162–63-10">[10]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Confucius" title="Confucius">Confucius</a> was a defining figure in ancient Chinese <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ancient_Chinese_philosophy" title="Ancient Chinese philosophy">philosophy</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ancient_Chinese_political_systems" title="Ancient Chinese political systems">politics</a>. He collected the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Six_Classics" title="Six Classics">Six Classics</a> as founding texts of <a href="/wiki/Confucianism" title="Confucianism">Confucianism</a>, and they became the central texts by which other works were compared in Chinese literary scholarship. Confucianism dominated literary tastes in Ancient China starting in the <a href="/wiki/Warring_States_period" title="Warring States period">Warring States period</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_11-0"><a href="#cite_note-:1-11">[11]</a></sup> The sayings of Confucius were later compiled into the <i><a href="/wiki/Analects" title="Analects">Analects</a></i> by his students.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELuo201164_12-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELuo201164-12">[12]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Anthology" title="Anthology">Anthologies</a> were common in Ancient China, and anthologizing was used as a means of literary criticism to determine literary classics.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_11-1"><a href="#cite_note-:1-11">[11]</a></sup> The <i><a href="/wiki/Classic_of_Poetry" title="Classic of Poetry">Classic of Poetry</a></i>, one of the Six Classics, is the oldest existing anthology of Chinese poetry. It comprises 305 works by anonymous authors dating from the 12th to 7th centuries BC. Prior to the collection of these works, poetic tradition in Ancient China was primarily oral.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup> The <i><a href="/wiki/Chu_Ci" title="Chu Ci">Chu Ci</a></i> anthology is a volume of poems from the Warring States period written in Chu and attributed to <a href="/wiki/Qu_Yuan" title="Qu Yuan">Qu Yuan</a>. These poems were written as rhapsodies that were meant to be recited with a specific tone rather than sung.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELuo201131–35_14-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELuo201131–35-14">[14]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Music_Bureau" title="Music Bureau">Music Bureau</a> was developed during the Zhou dynasty, establishing a governmental role for the collection of musical works and folk songs that would persist throughout Chinese history.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELuo2011137_15-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELuo2011137-15">[15]</a></sup>
</p><p>Historical documents developed into an early form of literature during the Warring States period, as documentation was combined with narrative and sometimes with legendary accounts of history.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELuo201111–12_16-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELuo201111–12-16">[16]</a></sup> Two of the Six Classics, the <i><a href="/wiki/Book_of_Documents" title="Book of Documents">Book of Documents</a></i> and the <i><a href="/wiki/Spring_and_Autumn_Annals" title="Spring and Autumn Annals">Spring and Autumn Annals</a></i>, are historical documents. The latter inspired works of historical commentary that became a genre in their own right, including the <i><a href="/wiki/Zuo_Zhuan" title="Zuo Zhuan">Zuo Zhuan</a></i>, the <i><a href="/wiki/Gongyang_Zhuan" title="Gongyang Zhuan">Gongyang Zhuan</a></i>, and the <i><a href="/wiki/Guliang_Zhuan" title="Guliang Zhuan">Guliang Zhuan</a></i>. The <i>Zuo Zhuan</i> is considered to be the first large scale narrative work in Chinese literature.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELuo201151–55_17-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELuo201151–55-17">[17]</a></sup> <i><a href="/wiki/The_Art_of_War" title="The Art of War">The Art of War</a></i> by <a href="/wiki/Sun_Tzu" title="Sun Tzu">Sun Tzu</a> was an influential book on military strategy that is still referenced in the modern era.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Qin_and_Han_dynasties">Qin and Han dynasties</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_literature&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Qin and Han dynasties">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Han_Dynasty_poetry" title="Han Dynasty poetry">Han Dynasty poetry</a></div>
<p>Poetry written in the brief period of the <a href="/wiki/Qin_dynasty" title="Qin dynasty">Qin dynasty</a> has been entirely lost. Poetry in the <a href="/wiki/Han_dynasty" title="Han dynasty">Han dynasty</a> diverged as several branches developed, including short length, paralleled exposition, rhymed exposition, and ancient style, and <a href="/wiki/Idealism" title="Idealism">idealism</a> also became popular during the Han dynasty.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_11-2"><a href="#cite_note-:1-11">[11]</a></sup> The <i><a href="/wiki/Nineteen_Old_Poems" title="Nineteen Old Poems">Nineteen Old Poems</a></i> were written at this time, though how they came about is the subject of debate.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELuo2011131_19-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELuo2011131-19">[19]</a></sup> Poetry during this period abandoned tetrasyllabic verse in favor of pentasyllabic verse. The ballads of Chu spread through China and became widely popular, often focusing on concepts of inevitable destiny and fate.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELuo2011125–127_20-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELuo2011125–127-20">[20]</a></sup>
</p><p>Political and argumentative literature by government officials dominated Chinese prose during this period, though even these works often engaged in lyricism and metaphor. <a href="/wiki/Jia_Yi" title="Jia Yi">Jia Yi</a> was an essayist known for his emotional political treatises such as <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/The_Faults_of_Qin" title="The Faults of Qin">The Faults of Qin</a></i>. <a href="/wiki/Chao_Cuo" title="Chao Cuo">Chao Cuo</a> was an essayist known for treatises that were meticulous rather than emotional. Confucianism continued to dictate philosophical works, though a movement of works criticizing contemporary application of Confucianism began with <a href="/wiki/Wang_Chong" title="Wang Chong">Wang Chong</a> in his <i><a href="/wiki/Lunheng" title="Lunheng">Lunheng</a></i>. Prose literature meant for entertainment also developed during this period.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELuo2011101–105_21-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELuo2011101–105-21">[21]</a></sup> Historical literature was revolutionized by the <i><a href="/wiki/Records_of_the_Grand_Historian" title="Records of the Grand Historian">Records of the Grand Historian</a></i>, the first general history of ancient times and the largest work of literature to that point in time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELuo2011113_22-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELuo2011113-22">[22]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Six_Dynasties">Six Dynasties</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_literature&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Six Dynasties">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Six_Dynasties_poetry" title="Six Dynasties poetry">Six Dynasties poetry</a></div>
<p>Centralism declined during the Six Dynasties period, and Confucianism lost influence as a predominating ideology. This caused the rise of many local traditions of philosophical literature, including that of Taoist and Buddhist ideas.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELuo2011149–150_23-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELuo2011149–150-23">[23]</a></sup> Prose fiction during the Wei and Jin dynasties consisted mainly of supernatural folklore, including those presented as historical.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELuo2011189–190_24-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELuo2011189–190-24">[24]</a></sup> This tradition of supernatural fiction continued during the Northern and Southern Dynasties with the <i>Records of Light and Shade</i> attributed to <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Liu_Yiqing&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Liu Yiqing (page does not exist)">Liu Yiqing</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELuo2011261_25-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELuo2011261-25">[25]</a></sup> Another genre of prose was collections of short biographical or anecdotal impressions, of which only <i><a href="/wiki/A_New_Account_of_the_Tales_of_the_World" title="A New Account of the Tales of the World">A New Account of the Tales of the World</a></i> survives.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELuo2011263_26-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELuo2011263-26">[26]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Jian%27an_poetry" title="Jian'an poetry">Jian'an poetry</a> developed from the literary tradition of Eastern Han, incorporating idiosyncrasies and strong demonstrations of emotion to express individualism. This movement was led by then-ruler of China <a href="/wiki/Cao_Cao" title="Cao Cao">Cao Cao</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Poetry_of_Cao_Cao" title="Poetry of Cao Cao">poetry of Cao Cao</a> consisted of ensemble songs published through the Music Bureau and performed with music.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELuo2011152–154_27-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELuo2011152–154-27">[27]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Seven_Sages_of_the_Bamboo_Grove" title="Seven Sages of the Bamboo Grove">Seven Sages of the Bamboo Grove</a> were influential poets in the Wei dynasty mid-3rd century, addressing political and philosophical concerns directly in their poetry.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELuo2011167_28-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELuo2011167-28">[28]</a></sup> Chinese poetry developed significantly during the <a href="/wiki/Jin_dynasty_(266%E2%80%93420)" title="Jin dynasty (266–420)">Jin dynasty</a>, incorporating <a href="/wiki/Parallelism_(rhetoric)" title="Parallelism (rhetoric)">parallelism</a>, <a href="/wiki/Prosody_(linguistics)" title="Prosody (linguistics)">prosody</a>, and emotional expression through scenery. <a href="/wiki/Zhang_Hua" title="Zhang Hua">Zhang Hua</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lu_Ji_(Shiheng)" title="Lu Ji (Shiheng)">Lu Ji</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Pan_Yue_(poet)" title="Pan Yue (poet)">Pan Yue</a> are recognized as the great poets that developed early Western Jin poetry.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELuo2011173_29-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELuo2011173-29">[29]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Zuo_Si" title="Zuo Si">Zuo Si</a> and <a href="/wiki/Liu_Kun_(Jin_dynasty)" title="Liu Kun (Jin dynasty)">Liu Kun</a> were poets in later Western Jin.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELuo2011178–180_30-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELuo2011178–180-30">[30]</a></sup> In Eastern Jin, philosophical poetry went through a period of abstraction that removed much of its literary elements. <a href="/wiki/Guo_Pu" title="Guo Pu">Guo Pu</a> and <a href="/wiki/Tao_Yuanming" title="Tao Yuanming">Tao Yuanming</a> were notable poets in Eastern Jin.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELuo2011181–184_31-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELuo2011181–184-31">[31]</a></sup>
</p><p>The popularity of literary poetry and <a href="/wiki/Aestheticism" title="Aestheticism">aestheticism</a> grew during the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Southern_dynasties" title="Southern dynasties">Southern Dynasties</a>, and literature as art began to be recognized as distinct from political and philosophical literature.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELuo2011194_32-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELuo2011194-32">[32]</a></sup> This resulted in the growth of literary criticism, with <i><a href="/wiki/The_Literary_Mind_and_the_Carving_of_Dragons" title="The Literary Mind and the Carving of Dragons">The Literary Mind and the Carving of Dragons</a></i> and <a href="/wiki/Zhong_Rong" title="Zhong Rong"><i>Ranking of Poetry</i></a> being written at this time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELuo2011197–201_33-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELuo2011197–201-33">[33]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Sixteen_Kingdoms" title="Sixteen Kingdoms">Sixteen Kingdoms</a> of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Northern_Dynasties" title="Northern Dynasties">Northern Dynasties</a> saw little cultural growth due to their instability, and Northern literature of this time was typically influenced by the Southern Dynasties.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELuo2011233_34-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELuo2011233-34">[34]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Shanshui_poetry" title="Shanshui poetry">Shanshui poetry</a> also became prominent in Six Dynasties poetry.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_11-3"><a href="#cite_note-:1-11">[11]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Levant">Levant</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_literature&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Levant">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Canaanite_and_Aramaic_inscriptions" title="Canaanite and Aramaic inscriptions">Canaanite and Aramaic inscriptions</a></div>
<p>Ancient literature of the Levant was written in the <a href="/wiki/Northwest_Semitic_languages" title="Northwest Semitic languages">Northwest Semitic languages</a>, a language group that contains the <a href="/wiki/Aramaic" title="Aramaic">Aramaic language</a>, as well as the <a href="/wiki/Canaanite_languages" title="Canaanite languages">Canaanite languages</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Phoenician_language" title="Phoenician language">Phoenician</a> and <a href="/wiki/Hebrew_language" title="Hebrew language">Hebrew</a>. A corpus of <a href="/wiki/Canaanite_and_Aramaic_inscriptions" title="Canaanite and Aramaic inscriptions">Canaanite and Aramaic inscriptions</a> (or "Northwest Semitic inscriptions") are the primary extra-Biblical source for the writings of the ancient <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Phoenicians" title="Phoenicians">Phoenicians</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ancient_Hebrews" title="Ancient Hebrews">Hebrews</a> and <a href="/wiki/Arameans" title="Arameans">Arameans</a>. These inscriptions occur on stone slabs, pottery <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ostraca" title="Ostraca">ostraca</a>, ornaments, and range from simple names to full texts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Woolmer_35-0"><a href="#cite_note-Woolmer-35">[35]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PR_36-0"><a href="#cite_note-PR-36">[36]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Suder1984_37-0"><a href="#cite_note-Suder1984-37">[37]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Doak2019_38-0"><a href="#cite_note-Doak2019-38">[38]</a></sup>
</p><p>The books that constitute the <a href="/wiki/Hebrew_Bible" title="Hebrew Bible">Hebrew Bible</a> developed over roughly a millennium, with the oldest texts originating from about the eleventh or tenth centuries BCE. They are edited works, being collections of various sources intricately and carefully woven together. The Old Testament was compiled and edited by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Authorship_of_the_Old_Testament" title="Authorship of the Old Testament">various authors</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup> over a period of centuries, with many scholars concluding that the Hebrew canon was <a href="/wiki/Development_of_the_Hebrew_Bible_canon" title="Development of the Hebrew Bible canon">solidified</a> by about the 3rd century BC.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Philip_R_page_50_41-0"><a href="#cite_note-Philip_R_page_50-41">[41]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/New_Testament" title="New Testament">New Testament</a> was an additional collection of books that supplemented the Hebrew Bible, consisting of the <a href="/wiki/Gospel" title="Gospel">gospels</a> that described <a href="/wiki/Jesus" title="Jesus">Jesus</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Epistle" title="Epistle">epistles</a> written by notable figures of early <a href="/wiki/Christianity" title="Christianity">Christianity</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Classical_antiquity">Classical antiquity</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_literature&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Classical antiquity">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Ancient_Greece">Ancient Greece</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_literature&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Ancient Greece">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Greek_literature" title="Ancient Greek literature">Ancient Greek literature</a></div><div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Bardo(js)014(js).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="723" data-file-width="1200" decoding="async" height="133" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ae/Bardo%28js%29014%28js%29.jpg/220px-Bardo%28js%29014%28js%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ae/Bardo%28js%29014%28js%29.jpg/330px-Bardo%28js%29014%28js%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ae/Bardo%28js%29014%28js%29.jpg/440px-Bardo%28js%29014%28js%29.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Bardo(js)014(js).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A scene from the <i>Odyssey</i> portrayed in an Ancient Roman mosaic</div></div></div>
<p>Early Greek literature was composed in <a href="/wiki/Dactylic_hexameter" title="Dactylic hexameter">dactylic hexameter</a>. <a href="/wiki/Homer" title="Homer">Homer</a> is credited with the codification of epic poetry in Ancient Greece with the <i><a href="/wiki/Iliad" title="Iliad">Iliad</a></i> and the <i><a href="/wiki/Odyssey" title="Odyssey">Odyssey</a>.</i> <a href="/wiki/Hesiod" title="Hesiod">Hesiod</a> is credited with developing a literary tradition of poetry derived from catalogues and genealogies, such as the <i><a href="/wiki/Megala_Erga" title="Megala Erga">Megala Erga</a></i> and the <i><a href="/wiki/Theogony" title="Theogony">Theogony</a></i>. Notable writers of religious literature also held similar prominence at the time, but these works have since been lost.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup> Notable among later Greek poets was <a href="/wiki/Sappho" title="Sappho">Sappho</a>, who contributed to the development of <a href="/wiki/Lyric_poetry" title="Lyric poetry">lyric poetry</a> and was widely popular in antiquity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Theatre_of_ancient_Greece" title="Theatre of ancient Greece">Ancient Greek plays</a> originate from the <a href="/wiki/Greek_chorus" title="Greek chorus">chorus plays</a> of <a href="/wiki/Athens" title="Athens">Athens</a> in the 6th century BC as a tradition to honor <a href="/wiki/Dionysus" title="Dionysus">Dionysus</a>, the god of theater and wine. Greek plays came to be associated with "elaborate costumes, complex choreography, scenic architecture, and the mask". They were often structured as a <a href="/wiki/Tetralogy" title="Tetralogy">tetralogy</a> in which three tragedies were followed by a <a href="/wiki/Satyr_play" title="Satyr play">satyr play</a>. <a href="/wiki/Aeschylus" title="Aeschylus">Aeschylus</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sophocles" title="Sophocles">Sophocles</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Euripides" title="Euripides">Euripides</a> were known for their <a href="/wiki/Tragedy" title="Tragedy">tragedies</a>, while <a href="/wiki/Aristophanes" title="Aristophanes">Aristophanes</a> and <a href="/wiki/Menander" title="Menander">Menander</a> were known for their <a href="/wiki/Comedy_(drama)" title="Comedy (drama)">comedies</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup> Sophocles is most well known for his play <i><a href="/wiki/Oedipus_Rex" title="Oedipus Rex">Oedipus Rex</a></i>, which established an early example of literary <a href="/wiki/Irony" title="Irony">irony</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESutherland200821–25_46-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESutherland200821–25-46">[46]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Ancient_Greek_philosophy" title="Ancient Greek philosophy">Ancient Greek philosophy</a> was developed as the foundation of <a href="/wiki/Western_philosophy" title="Western philosophy">Western philosophy</a>. <a href="/wiki/Thales_of_Miletus" title="Thales of Miletus">Thales of Miletus</a> was the first person in recorded history to engage in Western philosophy. The Ancient Greek philosophical literature was advanced by <a href="/wiki/Plato" title="Plato">Plato</a>, who incorporated philosophical debates into dialogues with <a href="/wiki/Socratic_questioning" title="Socratic questioning">Socratic questioning</a>. <a href="/wiki/Aristotle" title="Aristotle">Aristotle</a>, Plato's student, wrote dozens of works on many scientific disciplines.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup> Aristotle also developed early <a href="/wiki/Literary_criticism" title="Literary criticism">literary criticism</a> and <a href="/wiki/Literary_theory" title="Literary theory">literary theory</a> in his <i><a href="/wiki/Poetics_(Aristotle)" title="Poetics (Aristotle)">Poetics</a>.</i><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESutherland200821–25_46-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESutherland200821–25-46">[46]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Ancient_Rome">Ancient Rome</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_literature&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Ancient Rome">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Latin_literature" title="Latin literature">Latin literature</a></div>
<p>In the <a href="/wiki/Roman_Republic" title="Roman Republic">Roman Republic</a>, literature took the form of tragedy, comedy, epic, and historical. <a href="/wiki/Livius_Andronicus" title="Livius Andronicus">Livius Andronicus</a> is recognized as the originator of literature in the Latin language, and due to Rome's influence, the development of Latin literature often extended beyond the traditional boundaries of Rome. <a href="/wiki/Plautus" title="Plautus">Plautus</a> was an influential playwright known for his comedies that emphasized humor and <a href="/wiki/Popular_culture" title="Popular culture">popular culture</a>. The late republic saw the rise of <a href="/wiki/Augustan_literature_(ancient_Rome)" title="Augustan literature (ancient Rome)">Augustan literature</a> and <a href="/wiki/Classical_Latin" title="Classical Latin">Classical Latin</a>, which was primarily prose and included the works of <a href="/wiki/Cicero" title="Cicero">Cicero</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sallust" title="Sallust">Sallust</a>. Upon the formation of the <a href="/wiki/Roman_Empire" title="Roman Empire">Roman Empire</a>, political commentary declined and prose went out of favor to be replaced by poetry. Poets such as <a href="/wiki/Virgil" title="Virgil">Virgil</a>, <a href="/wiki/Horace" title="Horace">Horace</a>, <a href="/wiki/Propertius" title="Propertius">Propertius</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Ovid" title="Ovid">Ovid</a> are recognized as bringing about the Golden Age of Latin literature.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECohen201745–48_48-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECohen201745–48-48">[48]</a></sup> Virgil's epic poem the <i><a href="/wiki/Aeneid" title="Aeneid">Aeneid</a></i> closely followed the formula established by Homer.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECohen201754_49-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECohen201754-49">[49]</a></sup>
</p><p>Prominent Latin authors that lived during the early empire included <a href="/wiki/Pliny_the_Elder" title="Pliny the Elder">Pliny the Elder</a>, <a href="/wiki/Seneca_the_Younger" title="Seneca the Younger">Seneca the Younger</a>, and Emperor <a href="/wiki/Marcus_Aurelius" title="Marcus Aurelius">Marcus Aurelius</a>. As the Roman Empire grew, Latin literature increasingly came from Spain and Northern Africa. Historical works of the early empire included the epic <i><a href="/wiki/Pharsalia" title="Pharsalia">Pharsalia</a></i> by <a href="/wiki/Lucan" title="Lucan">Lucan</a>, which followed <a href="/wiki/Caesar%27s_civil_war" title="Caesar's civil war">Caesar's civil war</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Annals_(Tacitus)" title="Annals (Tacitus)"><i>Annals</i></a> of <a href="/wiki/Tacitus" title="Tacitus">Tacitus</a>, which recorded the events of the first century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECohen201757–63_50-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECohen201757–63-50">[50]</a></sup> <i><a href="/wiki/The_Golden_Ass" title="The Golden Ass">The Golden Ass</a></i> by <a href="/wiki/Apuleius" title="Apuleius">Apuleius</a> was written in the later Empire and is possibly the world's oldest novel.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECohen201765_51-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECohen201765-51">[51]</a></sup> The adoption of <a href="/wiki/Christianity" title="Christianity">Christianity</a> in the Roman Empire became apparent in Latin literature, most notably in the <a href="/wiki/Confessional_writing" title="Confessional writing">confessional writing</a> of <a href="/wiki/Augustine_of_Hippo" title="Augustine of Hippo">Augustine of Hippo</a>, such as the <i><a href="/wiki/Confessions_(Augustine)" title="Confessions (Augustine)">Confessions</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECohen201770–71_52-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECohen201770–71-52">[52]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Ancient_India">Ancient India</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_literature&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Ancient India">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Tamil_literature" title="Tamil literature">Tamil literature</a>, <a href="/wiki/Indian_literature" title="Indian literature">Indian literature</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kannada_literature" title="Kannada literature">Kannada literature</a>, <a href="/wiki/Telugu_literature" title="Telugu literature">Telugu literature</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Sanskrit_literature" title="Sanskrit literature">Sanskrit literature</a></div><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1097763485" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><table class="box-Unreferenced_section plainlinks metadata ambox ambox-content ambox-Unreferenced" role="presentation"><tbody><tr><td class="mbox-image"><div class="mbox-image-div"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Question_book-new.svg"><img alt="" data-file-height="399" data-file-width="512" decoding="async" height="39" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/50px-Question_book-new.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/75px-Question_book-new.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/100px-Question_book-new.svg.png 2x" width="50"/></a></div></td><td class="mbox-text"><div class="mbox-text-span">This section <b>does not <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citing_sources" title="Wikipedia:Citing sources">cite</a> any <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability">sources</a></b>.<span class="hide-when-compact"> Please help <a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=History_of_literature&amp;action=edit">improve this section</a> by <a href="/wiki/Help:Referencing_for_beginners" title="Help:Referencing for beginners">adding citations to reliable sources</a>. Unsourced material may be challenged and <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability#Burden_of_evidence" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability">removed</a>.</span> <span class="date-container"><i>(<span class="date">September 2022</span>)</i></span><span class="hide-when-compact"><i> (<small><a href="/wiki/Help:Maintenance_template_removal" title="Help:Maintenance template removal">Learn how and when to remove this template message</a></small>)</i></span></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>Knowledge traditions in India handed down philosophical gleanings and theological concepts through the two traditions of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Shruti" title="Shruti">Shruti</a> and <a href="/wiki/Smriti" title="Smriti">Smriti</a>, meaning <i>that which is learnt</i> and <i>that which is experienced</i>, which included the <a href="/wiki/Vedas" title="Vedas">Vedas</a>. It is generally believed that the <a href="/wiki/Puranas" title="Puranas">Puranas</a> are the earliest philosophical writings in Indian history, although linguistic works on <a href="/wiki/Sanskrit" title="Sanskrit">Sanskrit</a> existed earlier than 1000 BC. Puranic works such as the Indian epics: <a href="/wiki/Ramayana" title="Ramayana">Ramayana</a> and <i><a href="/wiki/Mahabharata" title="Mahabharata">Mahabharata</a></i>, have influenced countless other works, including Balinese <a href="/wiki/Kecak" title="Kecak">Kecak</a> and other performances such as shadow puppetry (<a href="/wiki/Wayang" title="Wayang">wayang</a>), and many European works. <a href="/wiki/Pali" title="Pali">Pali</a> literature has an important position in the rise of <a href="/wiki/Buddhism" title="Buddhism">Buddhism</a>. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Classical_Sanskrit_literature" title="Classical Sanskrit literature">Classical Sanskrit literature</a> flowers in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Maurya_period" title="Maurya period">Maurya</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gupta_period" title="Gupta period">Gupta periods</a>, roughly spanning the 2nd century BC to the 8th century AD. <a href="/wiki/Sangam_literature" title="Sangam literature">Classical Tamil literature</a> also emerged in the early historic period dating from 300 BC to 300 AD, and is the earliest secular literature of India, mainly dealing with themes such as love and war. The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gupta_period" title="Gupta period">Gupta period</a> in India sees the flowering of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sanskrit_drama" title="Sanskrit drama">Sanskrit drama</a>, classical <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sanskrit_poetry" title="Sanskrit poetry">Sanskrit poetry</a> and the compilation of the <a href="/wiki/Puranas" title="Puranas">Puranas</a>.
</p>
<h2><span id="Post-classical_.285th_century.E2.80.9315th_century.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Post-classical_(5th_century–15th_century)">Post-classical (5th century–15th century)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_literature&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Post-classical (5th century–15th century)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Early_medieval_literature" title="Early medieval literature">Early medieval literature</a> and <a href="/wiki/Medieval_literature" title="Medieval literature">Medieval literature</a></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Medieval_Europe">Medieval Europe</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_literature&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Medieval Europe">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>After the fall of Rome (in roughly 476), many of the literary approaches and styles invented by the Greeks and Romans fell out of favor in Europe. In the <a href="/wiki/Millennium" title="Millennium">millennium</a> or so that intervened between Rome's fall and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Florentine_Renaissance" title="Florentine Renaissance">Florentine Renaissance</a>, medieval literature focused more and more on faith and faith-related matters, in part because the works written by the Greeks had not been preserved in Europe, and therefore there were few models of classical literature to learn from and move beyond. Although much had been lost to the ravages of time (and to catastrophe, as in the burning of the Library of Alexandria), many Greek works remained extant: they were preserved and copied carefully by Muslim scribes. What little there was became changed and distorted, with new forms beginning to develop from the distortions. Some of these distorted beginnings of new styles can be seen in the literature generally described as <a href="/wiki/Matter_of_Rome" title="Matter of Rome">Matter of Rome</a>, <a href="/wiki/Matter_of_France" title="Matter of France">Matter of France</a> and <a href="/wiki/Matter_of_Britain" title="Matter of Britain">Matter of Britain</a>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (May 2010)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p>Around 400 AD, the <i>Prudenti <a href="/wiki/Psychomachia" title="Psychomachia">Psychomachia</a></i> began the tradition of allegorical tales. Poetry flourished, however, in the hands of the <a href="/wiki/Troubadour" title="Troubadour">troubadours</a>, whose courtly romances and <i><a href="/wiki/Chanson_de_geste" title="Chanson de geste">chanson de geste</a></i> amused and entertained the upper classes who were their patrons. The <a href="/wiki/First_Crusade" title="First Crusade">First Crusade</a> in 1095 also affected literature. For instance the image of the <a href="/wiki/Knight" title="Knight">knight</a> would take on a different significance. The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Islamic" title="Islamic">Islamic</a> emphasis on scientific investigation and the preservation of the Greek philosophical writings would also affect European literature.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (May 2010)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Hagiography" title="Hagiography">Hagiographies</a>, or "lives of the <a href="/wiki/List_of_saints" title="List of saints">saints</a>", were frequent among early medieval European texts. The writings of <a href="/wiki/Bede" title="Bede">Bede</a>—<i><a href="/wiki/Ecclesiastical_History_of_the_English_People" title="Ecclesiastical History of the English People">Historia ecclesiastica gentis Anglorum</a></i>—and others continue the faith-based historical tradition begun by Eusebius in the early 4th century. Between Augustine and <i>The Bible</i>, religious authors had numerous aspects of Christianity that needed further explication and interpretation. <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Aquinas" title="Thomas Aquinas">Thomas Aquinas</a>, more than any other single person, was able to turn <a href="/wiki/Theology" title="Theology">theology</a> into a kind of science, in part because he was heavily influenced by Aristotle, whose works were returning to Europe in the 13th century. Playwriting essentially ceased, except for the <a href="/wiki/Mystery_play" title="Mystery play">mystery plays</a> and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Passion_play" title="Passion play">passion plays</a> that focused heavily on conveying Christian belief to the common people.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (May 2010)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Latin" title="Latin">Latin</a> continued to be used as a <a href="/wiki/Literary_language" title="Literary language">literary language</a> in medieval Europe. Though it was also spoken, it was primarily learned and expressed through literature, and scientific literature was typically written in Latin.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup> Christianity became increasingly prominent in medieval European literature, also written in Latin. Religious literature in other languages proliferated during the 13th century as those who were not educated in Latin sought religious literature that they could understand. Women in particular were not permitted to learn Latin, and an extensive body of religious literature in many languages was written by women at this time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[54]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Medieval_England">Medieval England</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_literature&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Medieval England">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Early Medieval literature in England was written in <a href="/wiki/Old_English" title="Old English">Old English</a>, which is not mutually intelligible with modern English. Works of this time include the epic poem <i><a href="/wiki/Beowulf" title="Beowulf">Beowulf</a></i> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Arthurian_fantasy" title="Arthurian fantasy">Arthurian fantasy</a> based on the legendary character of <a href="/wiki/King_Arthur" title="King Arthur">King Arthur</a>. Literature in the modern English language began with <a href="/wiki/Geoffrey_Chaucer" title="Geoffrey Chaucer">Geoffrey Chaucer</a> in the 14th century, known for <i><a href="/wiki/The_Canterbury_Tales" title="The Canterbury Tales">The Canterbury Tales</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESutherland200826–28_55-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESutherland200826–28-55">[55]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Medieval_Italy">Medieval Italy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_literature&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Medieval Italy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p><i><a href="/wiki/The_Decameron" title="The Decameron">The Decameron</a></i> by <a href="/wiki/Giovanni_Boccaccio" title="Giovanni Boccaccio">Giovanni Boccaccio</a> was published in 1351, and it influenced European literature over the following centuries. Its framing device of ten individuals each telling ten stories introduced the term <i><a href="/wiki/Novella" title="Novella">novella</a></i> and inspired later works, including Chaucer's <i>Canterbury Tales</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESutherland200875–81_56-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESutherland200875–81-56">[56]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Islamic_world">Islamic world</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_literature&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Islamic world">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Islamic_literature" title="Islamic literature">Islamic literature</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Arabian_nights_manuscript.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="427" data-file-width="600" decoding="async" height="157" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/41/Arabian_nights_manuscript.jpg/220px-Arabian_nights_manuscript.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/41/Arabian_nights_manuscript.jpg/330px-Arabian_nights_manuscript.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/41/Arabian_nights_manuscript.jpg/440px-Arabian_nights_manuscript.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Arabian_nights_manuscript.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Arabic manuscript of the <i><a href="/wiki/One_Thousand_and_One_Nights" title="One Thousand and One Nights">One Thousand and One Nights</a></i>.</div></div></div>
<p>The most well known <a href="/wiki/Fiction" title="Fiction">fiction</a> from the Islamic world was <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/The_Book_of_One_Thousand_and_One_Nights" title="The Book of One Thousand and One Nights">The Book of One Thousand and One Nights</a></i> (<i>Arabian Nights</i>), which was a compilation of many earlier folk tales told by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Persian_Empire" title="Persian Empire">Persian</a> Queen <a href="/wiki/Scheherazade" title="Scheherazade">Scheherazade</a>. The epic took form in the 10th century and reached its final form by the 14th century; the number and type of tales have varied from one manuscript to another.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-arabianNights_57-0"><a href="#cite_note-arabianNights-57">[57]</a></sup> All Arabian <a href="/wiki/Fantasy" title="Fantasy">fantasy</a> tales were often called "Arabian Nights" when translated into <a href="/wiki/English_language" title="English language">English</a>, regardless of whether they appeared in <i>The Book of One Thousand and One Nights</i>, in any version, and a number of tales are known in Europe as "Arabian Nights" despite existing in no Arabic manuscript.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-arabianNights_57-1"><a href="#cite_note-arabianNights-57">[57]</a></sup> This epic has been influential in the West since it was translated in the 18th century, first by <a href="/wiki/Antoine_Galland" title="Antoine Galland">Antoine Galland</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[58]</a></sup> Many imitations were written, especially in France.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-arabianNights2_59-0"><a href="#cite_note-arabianNights2-59">[59]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Dante_Alighieri" title="Dante Alighieri">Dante Alighieri</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/Divine_Comedy" title="Divine Comedy">Divine Comedy</a></i>, considered the greatest epic of <a href="/wiki/Italian_literature" title="Italian literature">Italian literature</a>, derived many features of and episodes about the hereafter directly or indirectly from Arabic works on <a href="/wiki/Islamic_eschatology" title="Islamic eschatology">Islamic eschatology</a>: the <i><a href="/wiki/Hadith" title="Hadith">Hadith</a></i> and the <i><a href="/wiki/Kitab_al-Miraj" title="Kitab al-Miraj">Kitab al-Miraj</a></i> (translated into Latin in 1264 or shortly before<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Heullant_60-0"><a href="#cite_note-Heullant-60">[60]</a></sup> as <i>Liber Scale Machometi</i>, "The Book of Muhammad's Ladder") concerning <a href="/wiki/Muhammad" title="Muhammad">Muhammad</a>'s ascension to Heaven, and the spiritual writings of <a href="/wiki/Ibn_Arabi" title="Ibn Arabi">Ibn Arabi</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Moors" title="Moors">Moors</a> also had a noticeable influence on the works of <a href="/wiki/George_Peele" title="George Peele">George Peele</a> and <a href="/wiki/William_Shakespeare" title="William Shakespeare">William Shakespeare</a>. Some of their works featured Moorish characters, such as Peele's <i><a href="/wiki/The_Battle_of_Alcazar" title="The Battle of Alcazar">The Battle of Alcazar</a></i> and Shakespeare's <i><a href="/wiki/The_Merchant_of_Venice" title="The Merchant of Venice">The Merchant of Venice</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Titus_Andronicus" title="Titus Andronicus">Titus Andronicus</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Othello" title="Othello">Othello</a></i>, which featured a Moorish <a href="/wiki/Othello_(character)" title="Othello (character)">Othello</a> as its title character. These works are said to have been inspired by several Moorish <a href="/wiki/Delegation" title="Delegation">delegations</a> from <a href="/wiki/Morocco" title="Morocco">Morocco</a> to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Elizabethan_England" title="Elizabethan England">Elizabethan England</a> at the beginning of the 17th century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[61]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Arabic_literature">Arabic literature</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_literature&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Arabic literature">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Arabic_literature" title="Arabic literature">Arabic literature</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Literature_of_Morocco" title="Literature of Morocco">Literature of Morocco</a></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Ibn_Tufail" title="Ibn Tufail">Ibn Tufail</a> (Abubacer) and <a href="/wiki/Ibn_al-Nafis" title="Ibn al-Nafis">Ibn al-Nafis</a> (1213–1288) were pioneers of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Philosophical_novel" title="Philosophical novel">philosophical novel</a>. Ibn Tufail wrote the first fictional Arabic <a href="/wiki/Novel" title="Novel">novel</a> <i><a href="/wiki/Hayy_ibn_Yaqdhan" title="Hayy ibn Yaqdhan">Hayy ibn Yaqdhan</a></i> (<i>Philosophus Autodidactus</i>) as a response to <a href="/wiki/Al-Ghazali" title="Al-Ghazali">al-Ghazali</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/The_Incoherence_of_the_Philosophers" title="The Incoherence of the Philosophers">The Incoherence of the Philosophers</a></i>, and then Ibn al-Nafis also wrote a novel <i><a href="/wiki/Ibn_al-Nafis#Theologus_Autodidactus" title="Ibn al-Nafis">Theologus Autodidactus</a></i> as a response to Ibn Tufail's <i>Philosophus Autodidactus</i>. Both of these narratives had <a href="/wiki/Protagonist" title="Protagonist">protagonists</a> (Hayy in <i>Philosophus Autodidactus</i> and Kamil in <i>Theologus Autodidactus</i>) who were <a href="/wiki/Autodidacticism" title="Autodidacticism">autodidactic</a> <a href="/wiki/Feral_child" title="Feral child">feral children</a> living in seclusion on a <a href="/wiki/Desert_island" title="Desert island">desert island</a>, both being the earliest examples of a desert island story. However, while Hayy lives alone with animals on the desert island for the rest of the story in <i>Philosophus Autodidactus</i>, the story of Kamil extends beyond the desert island setting in <i>Theologus Autodidactus</i>, developing into the earliest known <a href="/wiki/Coming_of_age" title="Coming of age">coming of age</a> plot and eventually becoming the first example of a <a href="/wiki/Science_fiction" title="Science fiction">science fiction</a> novel.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[62]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[63]</a></sup>
</p><p><i>Theologus Autodidactus</i> deals with various science fiction elements such as <a href="/wiki/Abiogenesis" title="Abiogenesis">spontaneous generation</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Futurology" title="Futurology">futurology</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Eschatology" title="Eschatology">end of the world and doomsday</a>, <a href="/wiki/Resurrection" title="Resurrection">resurrection</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Afterlife" title="Afterlife">afterlife</a>. Rather than giving supernatural or mythological explanations for these events, Ibn al-Nafis attempted to explain these plot elements using the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Islamic_science" title="Islamic science">scientific knowledge</a> of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Islamic_medicine" title="Islamic medicine">biology</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Islamic_astronomy" title="Islamic astronomy">astronomy</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cosmology" title="Cosmology">cosmology</a> and <a href="/wiki/Geology" title="Geology">geology</a> known in his time. His main purpose behind this science fiction work was to explain <a href="/wiki/Islam" title="Islam">Islamic</a> religious teachings in terms of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Islamic_science" title="Islamic science">science</a> and <a href="/wiki/Early_Islamic_philosophy" title="Early Islamic philosophy">philosophy</a> through the use of fiction.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Roubi_64-0"><a href="#cite_note-Roubi-64">[64]</a></sup>
</p><p>A <a href="/wiki/Latin" title="Latin">Latin</a> translation of Ibn Tufail's work, <i>Philosophus Autodidactus</i>, first appeared in 1671, prepared by <a href="/wiki/Edward_Pococke" title="Edward Pococke">Edward Pococke</a> the Younger, followed by an English translation by <a href="/wiki/Simon_Ockley" title="Simon Ockley">Simon Ockley</a> in 1708, as well as <a href="/wiki/German_language" title="German language">German</a> and <a href="/wiki/Dutch_language" title="Dutch language">Dutch</a> translations. These translations later inspired <a href="/wiki/Daniel_Defoe" title="Daniel Defoe">Daniel Defoe</a> to write <i><a href="/wiki/Robinson_Crusoe" title="Robinson Crusoe">Robinson Crusoe</a></i>, a candidate for the title of "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/First_novel_in_English" title="First novel in English">first novel in English</a>".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[65]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[66]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Amber_67-0"><a href="#cite_note-Amber-67">[67]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wainwright_68-0"><a href="#cite_note-Wainwright-68">[68]</a></sup> <i>Philosophus Autodidactus</i> also inspired <a href="/wiki/Robert_Boyle" title="Robert Boyle">Robert Boyle</a> to write his own philosophical novel set on an island, <i>The Aspiring Naturalist</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Toomer-222_69-0"><a href="#cite_note-Toomer-222-69">[69]</a></sup> The story also anticipated <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rousseau" title="Rousseau">Rousseau</a>'s <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Emile:_or,_On_Education" title="Emile: or, On Education">Emile: or, On Education</a></i> in some ways, and is also similar to <a href="/wiki/Mowgli" title="Mowgli">Mowgli</a>'s story in <a href="/wiki/Rudyard_Kipling" title="Rudyard Kipling">Rudyard Kipling</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/The_Jungle_Book" title="The Jungle Book">The Jungle Book</a></i> as well as <a href="/wiki/Tarzan" title="Tarzan">Tarzan</a>'s story, in that a baby is abandoned but taken care of and fed by a mother <a href="/wiki/Wolf" title="Wolf">wolf</a>. Among other innovations in Arabic literature was <a href="/wiki/Ibn_Khaldun" title="Ibn Khaldun">Ibn Khaldun</a>'s perspective on chronicling past events—by fully rejecting supernatural explanations, Khaldun essentially invented the scientific or sociological approach to history.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (May 2010)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Persian_literature">Persian literature</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_literature&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Persian literature">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Persian_literature" title="Persian literature">Persian literature</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pahlavi_literature" title="Pahlavi literature">Pahlavi literature</a></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Ferdowsi" title="Ferdowsi">Ferdowsi</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/Shahnameh" title="Shahnameh">Shahnameh</a></i>, the national epic of <a href="/wiki/Iran" title="Iran">Iran</a>, is a mythical and heroic retelling of <a href="/wiki/History_of_Iran" title="History of Iran">Persian history</a> and the longest epic poem ever written. From <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Persian_Empire" title="Persian Empire">Persian</a> culture the book which would, eventually, become the most famous in the west is the <i><a href="/wiki/Rubaiyat_of_Omar_Khayyam" title="Rubaiyat of Omar Khayyam">Rubaiyat of Omar Khayyam</a></i>. The Rubáiyát is a collection of poems by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Persian_people" title="Persian people">Persian</a> mathematician and astronomer <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Omar_Khayy%C3%A1m" title="Omar Khayyám">Omar Khayyám</a> (1048–1122). "Rubaiyat" means "quatrains": verses of four lines. <i><a href="/wiki/Amir_Arsalan" title="Amir Arsalan">Amir Arsalan</a></i> was also a popular mythical Persian story, which has influenced some modern works of fantasy fiction, such as <i><a href="/wiki/The_Heroic_Legend_of_Arslan" title="The Heroic Legend of Arslan">The Heroic Legend of Arslan</a></i>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (August 2022)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p>Examples of early Persian proto-<a href="/wiki/Science_fiction" title="Science fiction">science fiction</a> include <a href="/wiki/Al-Farabi" title="Al-Farabi">Al-Farabi</a>'s <i>Opinions of the residents of a splendid city</i> about a <a href="/wiki/Utopia" title="Utopia">utopian</a> society, and elements such as the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Flying_carpet" title="Flying carpet">flying carpet</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[70]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Ottoman_literature">Ottoman literature</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_literature&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Ottoman literature">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ottoman_literature" title="Ottoman literature">Ottoman literature</a></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Poetry_of_the_Ottoman_Empire" title="Poetry of the Ottoman Empire">Poetry of the Ottoman Empire</a> and <a href="/wiki/Prose_of_the_Ottoman_Empire" title="Prose of the Ottoman Empire">Prose of the Ottoman Empire</a></div>
<p>The two primary streams of Ottoman written literature are <a href="/wiki/Poetry" title="Poetry">poetry</a> and <a href="/wiki/Prose" title="Prose">prose</a>. Of the two, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Divan_poetry" title="Divan poetry">divan poetry</a> was by far the dominant stream. Until the 19th century, Ottoman prose did not contain any examples of <a href="/wiki/Fiction" title="Fiction">fiction</a>; that is, there were no counterparts to, for instance, the European <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Romance_(heroic_literature)" title="Romance (heroic literature)">romance</a>, <a href="/wiki/Short_story" title="Short story">short story</a>, or novel (though analogous genres did, to some extent, exist in both the Turkish folk tradition and in divan poetry). Until the 19th century, Ottoman prose never managed to develop to the extent that contemporary divan poetry did. A large part of the reason for this was that much prose was expected to adhere to the rules of <i>sec'</i> (سجع, also transliterated as <i>seci</i>), or <a href="/wiki/Rhymed_prose" title="Rhymed prose">rhymed prose</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[71]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability"><span title="The material near this tag failed verification of its source citation(s). (August 2022)">failed verification</span></a></i>]</sup> a type of writing descended from the Arabic <i><a href="/wiki/Saj%27" title="Saj'">saj'</a></i> and which prescribed that between each adjective and noun in a sentence, there must be a <a href="/wiki/Rhyme" title="Rhyme">rhyme</a>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (August 2022)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Post-classical_Hebrew_literature">Post-classical Hebrew literature</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_literature&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Post-classical Hebrew literature">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Jewish_literature" title="Jewish literature">Jewish literature</a> and <a href="/wiki/Hebrew_literature" title="Hebrew literature">Hebrew literature</a></div>
<p>Medieval Jewish fiction often drew on ancient <a href="/wiki/Aggadah" title="Aggadah">Jewish legends</a>, and was written in a variety of languages including <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hebrew" title="Hebrew">Hebrew</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Judeo-Arabic" title="Judeo-Arabic">Judeo-Arabic</a>. <a href="/wiki/Piyyut" title="Piyyut">Liturgical Jewish poetry</a> in Hebrew flourished in Palestine in the seventh and eighth centuries with the writings of <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Yose_ben_Yose&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Yose ben Yose (page does not exist)">Yose ben Yose</a>, <a href="/wiki/Yanai_(Payetan)" title="Yanai (Payetan)">Yanai</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Eleazar_Kalir" title="Eleazar Kalir">Eleazar Kalir</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[72]</a></sup> Later Jewish poets in Spain, Provençal, and Italy wrote both religious and secular poems in Hebrew; particularly prominent poets were the Spanish Jewish poets <a href="/wiki/Solomon_ibn_Gabirol" title="Solomon ibn Gabirol">Solomon ibn Gabirol</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Yehuda_Halevi" title="Yehuda Halevi">Yehuda Halevi</a>. In addition to poetry and fiction, medieval Jewish literature also includes <a href="/wiki/Jewish_philosophy#Jewish_philosophy_before_Maimonides" title="Jewish philosophy">philosophical literature</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kabbalah:_Primary_Texts#Primary_texts" title="Kabbalah: Primary Texts">mystical (Kabbalistic) literature</a>, <a href="/wiki/Musar_literature#Medieval_Musar_literature" title="Musar literature">ethical (musar) literature</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Halakhic" title="Halakhic">legal (halakhic)</a> literature, and <a href="/wiki/Jewish_commentaries_on_the_Bible#Commentaries" title="Jewish commentaries on the Bible">commentaries on the Bible</a>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (August 2022)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Medieval_India">Medieval India</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_literature&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Medieval India">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Indian_literature" title="Indian literature">Indian literature</a></div>
<p>Sanskrit declines in the early 2nd millennium, late works such as the <i><a href="/wiki/Kathasaritsagara" title="Kathasaritsagara">Kathasaritsagara</a></i> dating to the 11th century, to the benefit of literature composed in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Middle_Indic" title="Middle Indic">Middle Indic</a> vernaculars such as <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Old_Bengali&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Old Bengali (page does not exist)">Old Bengali</a>, <a href="/wiki/Old_Hindi" title="Old Hindi">Old Hindi</a>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (August 2022)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Mid-imperial_China">Mid-imperial China</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_literature&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Mid-imperial China">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Chinese_literature" title="Chinese literature">Chinese literature</a></div>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Sui_and_Tang_dynasties">Sui and Tang dynasties</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_literature&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Sui and Tang dynasties">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
