<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:102px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:LiBai.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="654" data-file-width="395" decoding="async" height="166" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4c/LiBai.jpg/100px-LiBai.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4c/LiBai.jpg/150px-LiBai.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4c/LiBai.jpg/200px-LiBai.jpg 2x" width="100"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:LiBai.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Li Po Chanting a Poem, by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Liang_K%27ai" title="Liang K'ai">Liang K'ai</a> (13th century)</div></div></div><p><a href="/wiki/Lu_Sidao" title="Lu Sidao">Lu Sidao</a>, <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Xue_Daoheng&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Xue Daoheng (page does not exist)">Xue Daoheng</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Yang_Su" title="Yang Su">Yang Su</a> were notable poets of the early <a href="/wiki/Sui_dynasty" title="Sui dynasty">Sui dynasty</a>, with Yang moving away from the dominant traditions of Southern poetry.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELuo2011251–253_73-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELuo2011251–253-73">[73]</a></sup> In the Sui and early <a href="/wiki/Tang_dynasty" title="Tang dynasty">Tang</a> dynasties, literature was supported by the various emperors, who commissioned many works and wrote some of their own. Poetry in this period followed the Palace Style until it diverged with the work of the <a href="/wiki/Four_Paragons_of_the_Early_Tang" title="Four Paragons of the Early Tang">Four Paragons</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELuo2011268–275_74-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELuo2011268–275-74">[74]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Wang_Changling" title="Wang Changling">Wang Changling</a> and <a href="/wiki/Li_Bai" title="Li Bai">Li Bai</a> are recognized among the great poets of High Tang.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELuo2011282_75-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELuo2011282-75">[75]</a></sup> Landscape poetry and frontier poetry were both influential during the Tang dynasty.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELuo2011285–294_76-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELuo2011285–294-76">[76]</a></sup> Tang poetry also included <a href="/wiki/C%C3%AD_(poetry)" title="Cí (poetry)"><i>cí</i></a>, a type of lyric poetry.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELuo2011411_77-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELuo2011411-77">[77]</a></sup> Chinese poetry increased focus on politics, human suffering, and realism in the mid-Tang dynasty, such as in the works of <a href="/wiki/Du_Fu" title="Du Fu">Du Fu</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELuo2011310–311_78-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELuo2011310–311-78">[78]</a></sup> Chinese poetry diverged into two schools in the early-9th century; poets such as <a href="/wiki/Meng_Jiao" title="Meng Jiao">Meng Jiao</a> and <a href="/wiki/Han_Yu" title="Han Yu">Han Yu</a> wrote about the unusual, while poets such as <a href="/wiki/Bai_Juyi" title="Bai Juyi">Bai Juyi</a> and <a href="/wiki/Yuan_Zhen" title="Yuan Zhen">Yuan Zhen</a> emphasized simplicity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELuo2011334_79-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELuo2011334-79">[79]</a></sup> The final years of the Tang dynasty saw the rise of <a href="/wiki/Lyric_poetry" title="Lyric poetry">lyric poetry</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Erotic_poetry" title="Erotic poetry">erotic poetry</a>. <a href="/wiki/Li_Shangyin" title="Li Shangyin">Li Shangyin</a> and <a href="/wiki/Wen_Tingyun" title="Wen Tingyun">Wen Tingyun</a> were influential poets during this period.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELuo2011361–365_80-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELuo2011361–365-80">[80]</a></sup>
</p><p>Fictional narrative became prominent in the Tang dynasty, written with looser restrictions on form and length.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELuo2011391_81-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELuo2011391-81">[81]</a></sup> Fiction in the mid-Tang period focused primarily on social commentary and romantic love, and notable authors during this time included <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Shen_Jiji&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Shen Jiji (page does not exist)">Shen Jiji</a> and Yuan Zhen.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELuo2011396–397_82-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELuo2011396–397-82">[82]</a></sup> Collections of stories became more common in the Late Tang period, particularly those of chivalrous tales by authors such as <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Pei_Xing&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Pei Xing (page does not exist)">Pei Xing</a>. Popular literature of the time included transformation text, vernacular story, <a href="/wiki/Sutra" title="Sutra">sutra</a>, song, and rhapsody.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELuo2011403–405_83-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELuo2011403–405-83">[83]</a></sup> The style of prose was not initially developed during the Tang dynasty. Parallel prose remained popular in the early Tang dynasty, though writers such as Li Bai moved away from strict form that was common at the time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELuo2011305–308_84-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELuo2011305–308-84">[84]</a></sup> Han Yu promoted the use of classical prose in the style of ancient Confucisionist works.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELuo2011376–379_85-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELuo2011376–379-85">[85]</a></sup> Printing began in Tang Dynasty China. A copy of the <i><a href="/wiki/Diamond_Sutra" title="Diamond Sutra">Diamond Sutra</a></i>, a key <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Buddhist" title="Buddhist">Buddhist</a> text, found sealed in a cave in China in the early 20th century, is the oldest known dated printed book, with a printed date of 868. The method used was <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Block_printing" title="Block printing">block printing</a>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (August 2022)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Song_and_Jin_dynasties">Song and Jin dynasties</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_literature&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Song and Jin dynasties">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Printing first became widespread in the <a href="/wiki/Northern_Song_(960%E2%80%931127)" title="Northern Song (960–1127)">Northern Song</a>. Northern Song lyric poetry was developed by <a href="/wiki/Yan_Shu" title="Yan Shu">Yan Shu</a>, <a href="/wiki/Liu_Yong_(Song_dynasty)" title="Liu Yong (Song dynasty)">Liu Yong</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Zhang_Xian_(poet)" title="Zhang Xian (poet)">Zhang Xian</a>, and it became a popular pastime among the lower class.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELuo2011428–429_86-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELuo2011428–429-86">[86]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Ouyang_Xiu" title="Ouyang Xiu">Ouyang Xiu</a> developed the popular style of lyric poetry while <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Yan_Jidao&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Yan Jidao (page does not exist)">Yan Jidao</a> developed the refined style.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELuo2011439_87-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELuo2011439-87">[87]</a></sup> Lyric poetry contrasted with the more formal <a href="/wiki/Shi_(poetry)" title="Shi (poetry)"><i>shi</i></a> poetry that followed canonical literary forms and was used by scholars.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELuo2011428–429_86-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELuo2011428–429-86">[86]</a></sup> Political pressures heavily influenced the poetry of scholars in the Northern Song, as proficiency in older styles was a requirement for scholars to enter into civil service.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELuo2011461_88-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELuo2011461-88">[88]</a></sup> Politics and Confucianism in particular increasingly influenced poetry in Northern Song. Poets such as <a href="/wiki/Mei_Yaochen" title="Mei Yaochen">Mei Yaochen</a> and <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Su_Shunquin&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Su Shunquin (page does not exist)">Su Shunquin</a> developed the style of poetry used in the Middle Northern Song.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELuo2011474_89-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELuo2011474-89">[89]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Ouyang_Xiu" title="Ouyang Xiu">Ouyang Xiu</a> was a prominent literary scholar in Northern Song that refined the mainstream literary style of the time,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELuo2011480_90-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELuo2011480-90">[90]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Su_Shi" title="Su Shi">Su Shi</a> is said to have perfected it.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELuo2011490_91-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELuo2011490-91">[91]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Chen_Yuyi" title="Chen Yuyi">Chen Yuyi</a> defined the style of Early Southern Song poetry.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELuo2011508_92-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELuo2011508-92">[92]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Lu_You" title="Lu You">Lu You</a> was a poet in the Middle Southern Song that wrote extensively about political life in civil service and frustration with the dynasty's weakened position in the <a href="/wiki/Jin%E2%80%93Song_Wars" title="Jin–Song Wars">Jin–Song Wars</a>, while <a href="/wiki/Xin_Qiji" title="Xin Qiji">Xin Qiji</a> was a Middle Southern Song poet that wrote on similar topics from a military perspective.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELuo2011525–533_93-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELuo2011525–533-93">[93]</a></sup> Poetic style did not advance significantly in the Late Southern Song, though <a href="/wiki/Yan_Yu_(poetry_theorist)" title="Yan Yu (poetry theorist)">Yan Yu</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/Canglang_Shihua" title="Canglang Shihua">Canglang Shihua</a></i> was influential in poetic theory.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELuo2011539_94-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELuo2011539-94">[94]</a></sup> Classical poetry in the Early Jin emphasized emotion, while elegance was emphasized later in the Jin dynasty.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELuo2011563–564_95-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELuo2011563–564-95">[95]</a></sup>
</p><p>Popular fiction was typically performed in the Song dynasty, made up primarily of small talk fiction and historical tales. Classical prose fiction in the Song dynasty often sacrificed linguistic quality and imagination for plain language and moral teaching. <i><a href="/wiki/Zaju" title="Zaju">Zaju</a></i> variety plays developed during the Song dynasty as a predecessor to drama.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELuo2011557–562_96-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELuo2011557–562-96">[96]</a></sup> The scientist, statesman, and general <a href="/wiki/Shen_Kuo" title="Shen Kuo">Shen Kuo</a> (1031–1095 AD) was the author of the <i><a href="/wiki/Dream_Pool_Essays" title="Dream Pool Essays">Dream Pool Essays</a></i> (1088), a large book of scientific literature that included the oldest description of the magnetized <a href="/wiki/Compass" title="Compass">compass</a>. During the Song Dynasty, there was also the enormous historical work of the <i><a href="/wiki/Zizhi_Tongjian" title="Zizhi Tongjian">Zizhi Tongjian</a></i>, compiled into 294 volumes of 3 million written <a href="/wiki/Chinese_characters" title="Chinese characters">Chinese characters</a> by the year 1084 AD.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (August 2022)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> The <a href="/wiki/Jin_dynasty_(1115%E2%80%931234)" title="Jin dynasty (1115–1234)">Jin dynasty</a> saw advances in popular literature, including <i><a href="/wiki/Romance_of_the_Western_Chamber" title="Romance of the Western Chamber">Romance of the Western Chamber</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELuo2011507_97-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELuo2011507-97">[97]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Yuan_dynasty">Yuan dynasty</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_literature&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Yuan dynasty">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Drama was significantly developed as a literary form in the <a href="/wiki/Yuan_dynasty" title="Yuan dynasty">Yuan dynasty</a> and made up much of the era's fictional works.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELuo2011617_98-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELuo2011617-98">[98]</a></sup> Variety plays were influential in the Early Yuan period, with <a href="/wiki/Khanbaliq" title="Khanbaliq">Khanbaliq</a>, present-day <a href="/wiki/Beijing" title="Beijing">Beijing</a>, as the cultural center of variety plays.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELuo2011575_99-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELuo2011575-99">[99]</a></sup> As the 14th century began, variety play writers moved to <a href="/wiki/Hangzhou" title="Hangzhou">Hangzhou</a>, though variety plays declined and they did not achieve the same prominence.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELuo2011593_100-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELuo2011593-100">[100]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Nanxi_(theatre)" title="Nanxi (theatre)"><i>nanxi</i></a> was developed as a genre of play at the same time, reflecting the unique political life of the Yuan dynasty in which civil service, <a href="/wiki/Infidelity" title="Infidelity">infidelity</a>, and inter-clan politics all played a major role. <i><a href="/wiki/Tale_of_the_Pipa" title="Tale of the Pipa">Tale of the Pipa</a></i> by <a href="/wiki/Gao_Ming" title="Gao Ming">Gao Ming</a> was an influential <i>nanxi</i> drama.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELuo2011598–600_101-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELuo2011598–600-101">[101]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Qu_(poetry)" title="Qu (poetry)"><i>Qu</i></a> was a common type of poetry in the Yuan dynasty that was used both as a standalone work and part of the structure of a play.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELuo2011605_102-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELuo2011605-102">[102]</a></sup>
</p><p>Two of the earliest Chinese novels, <i><a href="/wiki/Romance_of_the_Three_Kingdoms" title="Romance of the Three Kingdoms">Romance of the Three Kingdoms</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Water_Margin" title="Water Margin">Water Margin</a></i>, first appeared in the Yuan dynasty.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELuo2011639_103-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELuo2011639-103">[103]</a></sup> Poetry in the Yuan dynasty remained the primary form of expression for classical writers, though the Song tradition of intellectual poetry was replaced by poetry that expressed strong emotion. Northern Yuan poetry was influenced by the works of <a href="/wiki/Yuan_Haowen" title="Yuan Haowen">Yuan Haowen</a> while Southern Yuan poetry was influenced by Yan Yu.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELuo2011617_98-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELuo2011617-98">[98]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Classical_and_feudal_Japan">Classical and feudal Japan</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_literature&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Classical and feudal Japan">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Japanese_literature" title="Japanese literature">Japanese literature</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/List_of_Japanese_classic_texts" title="List of Japanese classic texts">List of Japanese classic texts</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Booklet_of_A_Boat_Cast_Adrift_(Ukifune).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="399" data-file-width="600" decoding="async" height="146" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d7/Booklet_of_A_Boat_Cast_Adrift_%28Ukifune%29.jpg/220px-Booklet_of_A_Boat_Cast_Adrift_%28Ukifune%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d7/Booklet_of_A_Boat_Cast_Adrift_%28Ukifune%29.jpg/330px-Booklet_of_A_Boat_Cast_Adrift_%28Ukifune%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d7/Booklet_of_A_Boat_Cast_Adrift_%28Ukifune%29.jpg/440px-Booklet_of_A_Boat_Cast_Adrift_%28Ukifune%29.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Booklet_of_A_Boat_Cast_Adrift_(Ukifune).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Illustrated 13th century copy of <i><a href="/wiki/The_Tale_of_Genji" title="The Tale of Genji">The Tale of Genji</a></i></div></div></div>
<p>Classical Japanese literature generally refers to literature produced during the <a href="/wiki/Heian_period" title="Heian period">Heian period</a>. Japanese literature first diverged from Chinese literature around the eighth century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKato199712_104-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKato199712-104">[104]</a></sup> <i><a href="/wiki/Fudoki" title="Fudoki">Fudoki</a></i> were eighth century records that were typically written in Chinese and documented both historical and mythological stories.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKato199718_105-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKato199718-105">[105]</a></sup> Folk ballads were also common, including those recorded in the <i>fudoki</i> and musical ballads. These ballads were written to be chanted and often had a syllabic structure, with the <i><a href="/wiki/Tanka" title="Tanka">tanka</a></i> being highly regarded in particular.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKato199721_106-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKato199721-106">[106]</a></sup> The writing of <a href="/wiki/Waka_(poetry)" title="Waka (poetry)"><i>waka</i></a> poetry became increasingly important in the Heian period as it became a necessary skill for the aristocracy in both social and courtship settings.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKato199757–58_107-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKato199757–58-107">[107]</a></sup> The <i><a href="/wiki/Iroha" title="Iroha">iroha</a></i> poem, now one of two standard orderings for the Japanese <a href="/wiki/Syllabary" title="Syllabary">syllabary</a>, was also written during the early part of this period.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (August 2022)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p>The <i><a href="/wiki/Man%27y%C5%8Dsh%C5%AB" title="Man'yōshū">Man'yōshū</a></i> is the oldest collection of Japanese poetry, written in Japanese with Chinese characters through <i><a href="/wiki/Man%27y%C5%8Dgana" title="Man'yōgana">Man'yōgana</a></i> and compiling <i>waka</i> poetry from the fifth to eighth centuries.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKato199724_108-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKato199724-108">[108]</a></sup> The <i><a href="/wiki/Kokin_Wakash%C5%AB" title="Kokin Wakashū">Kokin Wakashū</a></i> was a collection of ninth century <i>waka</i> poetry compiled by imperial command. While the <i>Man'yōshū</i> was varied in the classes and professions of its writers, the poems of the <i>Kokin Wakashū</i> were limited to those of aristocratic poets.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKato199750–51_109-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKato199750–51-109">[109]</a></sup> <i><a href="/wiki/The_Tales_of_Ise" title="The Tales of Ise">The Tales of Ise</a></i> is a collection of loosely connected poems and narratives based on the life of <a href="/wiki/Ariwara_no_Narihira" title="Ariwara no Narihira">Ariwara no Narihira</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKato199748–49_110-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKato199748–49-110">[110]</a></sup> <i><a href="/wiki/The_Tale_of_the_Bamboo_Cutter" title="The Tale of the Bamboo Cutter">The Tale of the Bamboo Cutter</a></i> can be considered an early example of proto-<a href="/wiki/Science_fiction" title="Science fiction">science fiction</a>, written in the 10th century. The protagonist of the story is a princess from the Moon who is sent to Earth for safety during a celestial war, and a manuscript illustration depicts a disc-shaped flying object similar to a <a href="/wiki/Flying_saucer" title="Flying saucer">flying saucer</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Richardson_111-0"><a href="#cite_note-Richardson-111">[111]</a></sup>
</p><p><i><a href="/wiki/Utsubo_Monogatari" title="Utsubo Monogatari">Utsubo Monogatari</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Ochikubo_Monogatari" title="Ochikubo Monogatari">Ochikubo Monogatari</a></i> were early prose works from the 10th century that realistically portrayed the lives of the aristocracy, and the former is sometimes considered to be the first novel.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKato199762_112-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKato199762-112">[112]</a></sup> At the same time, women of the aristocracy began keeping diaries that followed aristocratic life.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKato199768_113-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKato199768-113">[113]</a></sup> <i><a href="/wiki/The_Tale_of_Genji" title="The Tale of Genji">The Tale of Genji</a></i> was the next major prose work in Japan, written in the 11th century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKato199772–73_114-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKato199772–73-114">[114]</a></sup> Its use of realism and romantic idealization inspired later works of Heian period prose fiction, including historical works such as <i><a href="/wiki/Eiga_Monogatari" title="Eiga Monogatari">Eiga Monogatari</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/%C5%8Ckagami" title="Ōkagami">Ōkagami</a></i>; romantic novels such as <i><a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=The_Tale_of_Sagoromo&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="The Tale of Sagoromo (page does not exist)">The Tale of Sagoromo</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Yoru_no_Nezame" title="Yoru no Nezame">Yoru no Nezame</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Hamamatsu_Ch%C5%ABnagon_Monogatari" title="Hamamatsu Chūnagon Monogatari">Hamamatsu Chūnagon Monogatari</a></i>, and <i><a href="/wiki/Torikaebaya_Monogatari" title="Torikaebaya Monogatari">Torikaebaya Monogatari</a></i>; and short story collections such as <i><a href="/wiki/Tsutsumi_Ch%C5%ABnagon_Monogatari" title="Tsutsumi Chūnagon Monogatari">Tsutsumi Chūnagon Monogatari</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKato199778_115-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKato199778-115">[115]</a></sup> While these stories typically portrayed the aristocracy, <i><a href="/wiki/Konjaku_Monogatarish%C5%AB" title="Konjaku Monogatarishū">Konjaku Monogatarishū</a></i> was written in the 12th century, compiling roughly one thousand stories from different walks of life in Japan, China, and India.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKato199784_116-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKato199784-116">[116]</a></sup> Japanese literature expanded beyond the aristocracy in the 13th century and became increasingly accessible to lower classes, often through the narration of religious texts such as <i><a href="/wiki/The_Tale_of_the_Heike" title="The Tale of the Heike">The Tale of the Heike</a></i> by <a href="/wiki/Biwa_h%C5%8Dshi" title="Biwa hōshi">blind priests</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKato1997100_117-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKato1997100-117">[117]</a></sup> In the 14th and 15th centuries, poetry such as <i><a href="/wiki/Renga" title="Renga">renga</a></i> and drama such as <i><a href="/wiki/Noh" title="Noh">noh</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Ky%C5%8Dgen" title="Kyōgen">kyōgen</a></i> was written by professional writers under the patronage of the court, temples, or local lords.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKato1997112_118-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKato1997112-118">[118]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Southeast_Asia">Southeast Asia</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_literature&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Southeast Asia">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The literary tradition of <a href="/wiki/Java" title="Java">Java</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Kawi_language" title="Kawi language">Kawi language</a> is most well known for <i><a href="/wiki/Kakawin" title="Kakawin">kakawin</a></i> poetry. These were narrative poems based on the traditions of <a href="/wiki/Sanskrit" title="Sanskrit">Sanskrit</a> poetry, and they often incorporated religious elements. The oldest surviving <i>kakawin</i> is <i><a href="/wiki/Kakawin_Ramayana" title="Kakawin Ramayana">Kakawin Ramayana</a></i> from the 9th century, a Javanese localization of the Sanskrit <i><a href="/wiki/Bha%E1%B9%AD%E1%B9%ADik%C4%81vya" title="Bhaṭṭikāvya">Bhaṭṭikāvya</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-119"><a href="#cite_note-119">[119]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Pre-Colombian_Mesoamerica">Pre-Colombian Mesoamerica</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_literature&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: Pre-Colombian Mesoamerica">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Mesoamerican_literature" title="Mesoamerican literature">Mesoamerican literature</a></div>
<p>Mesoamerican literature was typically recorded on <a href="/wiki/Codex" title="Codex">codices</a>, though most surviving codices of pre-Colombian literature were written in the Latin alphabet to preserve oral tradition after colonization. <a href="/wiki/Nahuatl" title="Nahuatl">Nahuatl</a> literature was divided into <span title="Nahuatl languages collective text"><i lang="nah">cuícatl</i></span>, which included song and poetry, and <span title="Nahuatl languages collective text"><i lang="nah">tlahtolli</i></span>, which included prose works of history and discourses. The <span title="Nahuatl languages collective text"><i lang="nah">teocuícatl</i></span> were divine hymns that were sung to praise the gods, while other Nahuatl poetry was sung in celebration of life and friendship, to honor warriors, or to pose philosophical questions. King <a href="/wiki/Nezahualcoyotl_(tlatoani)" title="Nezahualcoyotl (tlatoani)">Nezahualcoyotl</a> of <a href="/wiki/Tetzcoco_(altepetl)" title="Tetzcoco (altepetl)">Tetzcoco</a> was a notable poet and songwriter.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:3_120-0"><a href="#cite_note-:3-120">[120]</a></sup>
</p><p>Literature in the <a href="/wiki/Mayan_languages" title="Mayan languages">Mayan languages</a> was closely related to oral tradition in which writing guided memorized passages that were often performed. It was highly symbolic and incorporated heavy use of wordplay. Metaphor and imagery involving the natural world was also common. Mayan literature was often religious in nature, including information on religious practices, divination, and the gods. Much of this literature was later condemned as heretical and destroyed by Christian priests. The <a href="/wiki/Dresden_Codex" title="Dresden Codex">Dresden Codex</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Paris_Codex" title="Paris Codex">Paris Codex</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Madrid_Codex_(Maya)" title="Madrid Codex (Maya)">Madrid Codex</a> are the only surviving pre-Colombian Mayan codices. Notable surviving Mayan texts include the <a href="/wiki/Popol_Vuh" title="Popol Vuh">Popol Vuh</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Chilam_Balam" title="Chilam Balam">Chilam Balam</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Annals_of_the_Cakchiquels" title="Annals of the Cakchiquels">Annals of the Cakchiquels</a> that describe the religious beliefs of Mesoamerican cultures.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:3_120-1"><a href="#cite_note-:3-120">[120]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span id="Early_modern_.2815th_century.E2.80.9318th_century.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_modern_(15th_century–18th_century)">Early modern (15th century–18th century)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_literature&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: Early modern (15th century–18th century)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Early_Modern_literature" title="Early Modern literature">Early Modern literature</a></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/15th_century_in_literature" title="15th century in literature">15th century in literature</a>, <a href="/wiki/16th_century_in_literature" title="16th century in literature">16th century in literature</a>, and <a href="/wiki/17th_century_in_literature" title="17th century in literature">17th century in literature</a></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_modern_Europe">Early modern Europe</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_literature&amp;action=edit&amp;section=31" title="Edit section: Early modern Europe">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Western_literature" title="Western literature">Western literature</a></div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Renaissance" title="Renaissance">Renaissance</a> encompassed much of European culture during the early modern period. This period saw a renewed interested in the classical works of Ancient Greece and Rome and a proliferation of artistic and scientific achievement. Literature, as with most forms of art in the early modern period, was financed through <a href="/wiki/Patronage" title="Patronage">patronage</a> by nobles. Fiction writing was not considered a profession in its own right and was typically undertaken by those who already possessed independent wealth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:2_121-0"><a href="#cite_note-:2-121">[121]</a></sup> The invention of the <a href="/wiki/Printing_press" title="Printing press">printing press</a> in the mid-15th century revolutionized European literature. The production of printed books allowed for more uniformity in literary works and the spread of <a href="/wiki/Literacy" title="Literacy">literacy</a>. Religious literature in particular was affected by the printing press, as churches funded and involved themselves in the printing process. Literary criticism also developed as literary works became more accessible.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-122"><a href="#cite_note-122">[122]</a></sup>
</p><p>The form of writing now commonplace across the world—the <a href="/wiki/Novel" title="Novel">novel</a>—originated from the <a href="/wiki/Early_modern_period" title="Early modern period">early modern period</a> and grew in popularity in the next century. Before the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Modern_novel" title="Modern novel">modern novel</a> became established as a form there first had to be a transitional stage when "novelty" began to appear in the style of the epic poem. <a href="/wiki/Petrarch" title="Petrarch">Petrarch</a> popularized the <a href="/wiki/Sonnet" title="Sonnet">sonnet</a> as a poetic form; <a href="/wiki/Giovanni_Boccaccio" title="Giovanni Boccaccio">Giovanni Boccaccio</a>'s <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Decameron" title="Decameron">Decameron</a></i> made romance acceptable in prose as well as poetry; <a href="/wiki/Fran%C3%A7ois_Rabelais" title="François Rabelais">François Rabelais</a> rejuvenates satire with <i><a href="/wiki/Gargantua_and_Pantagruel" title="Gargantua and Pantagruel">Gargantua and Pantagruel</a></i>; <a href="/wiki/Michel_de_Montaigne" title="Michel de Montaigne">Michel de Montaigne</a> single-handedly invented the <a href="/wiki/Essay" title="Essay">essay</a> and used it to catalog his life and ideas. Perhaps the most controversial and important work of the time period was a treatise printed in <a href="/wiki/Nuremberg" title="Nuremberg">Nuremberg</a>, entitled <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/De_Revolutionibus_Orbium_Coelestium" title="De Revolutionibus Orbium Coelestium">De Revolutionibus Orbium Coelestium</a></i>: in it, the astronomer <a href="/wiki/Nicolaus_Copernicus" title="Nicolaus Copernicus">Nicolaus Copernicus</a> removed the Earth from its privileged position in the universe, which had far-reaching effects, not only in science, but in literature and its approach to humanity, hierarchy, and truth.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (August 2022)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p>Plays for entertainment (as opposed to religious enlightenment) returned to Europe's stages in the early modern period. <a href="/wiki/William_Shakespeare" title="William Shakespeare">William Shakespeare</a> is the most notable of the early modern playwrights, but numerous others made important contributions, including <a href="/wiki/Moli%C3%A8re" title="Molière">Molière</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pierre_Corneille" title="Pierre Corneille">Pierre Corneille</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jean_Racine" title="Jean Racine">Jean Racine</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pedro_Calder%C3%B3n_de_la_Barca" title="Pedro Calderón de la Barca">Pedro Calderón de la Barca</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lope_de_Vega" title="Lope de Vega">Lope de Vega</a>, <a href="/wiki/Christopher_Marlowe" title="Christopher Marlowe">Christopher Marlowe</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Ben_Jonson" title="Ben Jonson">Ben Jonson</a>. From the 16th to the 18th century <a href="/wiki/Commedia_dell%27arte" title="Commedia dell'arte">Commedia dell'arte</a> performers improvised in the streets of Italy and France. Some Commedia dell'arte plays were written down. Both the written plays and the <a href="/wiki/Improvisation" title="Improvisation">improvisation</a> were influential upon literature of the time, particularly upon the work of Molière. Shakespeare drew upon the arts of <a href="/wiki/Jester" title="Jester">jesters</a> and strolling players in creating new style comedies. All the parts, even the female ones, were played by men (<i><a href="/wiki/Drag_(clothing)" title="Drag (clothing)">en travesti</a></i>) but that would change, first in France and then in England too, by the end of the 17th century.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (August 2022)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="British_literature">British literature</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_literature&amp;action=edit&amp;section=32" title="Edit section: British literature">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/British_literature" title="British literature">British literature</a></div>
<p>As England grew to become a <a href="/wiki/Superpower" title="Superpower">world power</a>, focus on England itself appeared in English literature. This tradition began with <i><a href="/wiki/The_Faerie_Queene" title="The Faerie Queene">The Faerie Queene</a></i> by <a href="/wiki/Edmund_Spenser" title="Edmund Spenser">Edmund Spenser</a>, written through the 1590s in honor of Queen Elizabeth I.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESutherland200861_123-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESutherland200861-123">[123]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Metaphysical_poets" title="Metaphysical poets">Metaphysical poetry</a> developed in the 17th century, led by <a href="/wiki/John_Donne" title="John Donne">John Donne</a>, most well known for his love poems.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESutherland200854_124-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESutherland200854-124">[124]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Augustan_literature" title="Augustan literature">Augustan literature</a> movement developed in the 18th century, led by <a href="/wiki/Samuel_Johnson" title="Samuel Johnson">Samuel Johnson</a>, seeking to imitate the classical tastes of Ancient Greece and Rome. Augustan critics condemned metaphysical poetry for its frivolity and subversion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESutherland200857_125-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESutherland200857-125">[125]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/John_Milton" title="John Milton">John Milton</a> wrote the epic poem <i><a href="/wiki/Paradise_Lost" title="Paradise Lost">Paradise Lost</a></i> to create an English Christian epic that rivaled those of Homer and Virgil.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESutherland200864_126-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESutherland200864-126">[126]</a></sup> During the rule of <a href="/wiki/Oliver_Cromwell" title="Oliver Cromwell">Oliver Cromwell</a> in the 1650s, the arts were restricted along <a href="/wiki/Puritans" title="Puritans">Puritan</a> ideals. This censorship contributed to the fall of the <a href="/wiki/Commonwealth_of_England" title="Commonwealth of England">Commonwealth of England</a> and the restoration of the British monarchy. Under the restored monarchy, the arts were freer than they had been previously, and King <a href="/wiki/Charles_II_of_England" title="Charles II of England">Charles II</a> became a patron of the theater.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESutherland200875–81_56-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESutherland200875–81-56">[56]</a></sup>
</p><p>William Shakespeare was a famed playwright in the late-16th and early-17th centuries, and he is generally recognized as the greatest author in the English language. He is known for his comedies, his dramas, and his histories, as well as his use of <a href="/wiki/Blank_verse" title="Blank verse">blank verse</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sonnet" title="Sonnet">sonnet</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Soliloquy" title="Soliloquy">soliloquy</a>. Shakespeare's historical plays ignored historical accuracy and often contemplated <a href="/wiki/Order_of_succession" title="Order of succession">royal succession</a>, reflecting the concerns of his day regarding the succession of <a href="/wiki/Elizabeth_I" title="Elizabeth I">Elizabeth I</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESutherland200840–44_127-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESutherland200840–44-127">[127]</a></sup> Early English novels include <i><a href="/wiki/The_Pilgrim%27s_Progress" title="The Pilgrim's Progress">The Pilgrim's Progress</a></i> by <a href="/wiki/John_Bunyan" title="John Bunyan">John Bunyan</a> and <i><a href="/wiki/Oroonoko" title="Oroonoko">Oroonoko</a></i> by <a href="/wiki/Aphra_Behn" title="Aphra Behn">Aphra Behn</a>, both published in the 1680s. Bunyan introduced into English literature ideas of <a href="/wiki/Individualism" title="Individualism">individualism</a> and the quest of <a href="/wiki/Personal_fulfillment" title="Personal fulfillment">personal fulfillment</a>. Behn used her writing as <a href="/wiki/Social_criticism" title="Social criticism">social criticism</a> to question the <a href="/wiki/Atlantic_slave_trade" title="Atlantic slave trade">Atlantic slave trade</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESutherland200875–81_56-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESutherland200875–81-56">[56]</a></sup> <i><a href="/wiki/Robinson_Crusoe" title="Robinson Crusoe">Robinson Crusoe</a></i> by <a href="/wiki/Daniel_Defoe" title="Daniel Defoe">Daniel Defoe</a> is considered to be the first modern English novel, written as a celebration of the <a href="/wiki/Social_mobility" title="Social mobility">social mobility</a> introduced by <a href="/wiki/Capitalism" title="Capitalism">capitalism</a>. The book was written in a <a href="/wiki/Realism_(arts)" title="Realism (arts)">realistic</a> style, and the original edition was marketed as a true autobiography without any mention of Defoe as an author. <a href="/wiki/Jonathan_Swift" title="Jonathan Swift">Jonathan Swift</a> introduced the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fantasy_novel" title="Fantasy novel">fantasy novel</a> to English literature, most notably through <a href="/wiki/Gulliver%27s_Travels" title="Gulliver's Travels">Gulliver's Travels</a>, which was similarly marketed as a true story.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESutherland200882–87_128-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESutherland200882–87-128">[128]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="French_literature">French literature</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_literature&amp;action=edit&amp;section=33" title="Edit section: French literature">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/French_literature" title="French literature">French literature</a></div><p><i><a href="/wiki/Gargantua_and_Pantagruel" title="Gargantua and Pantagruel">Gargantua and Pantagruel</a></i> was written by <a href="/wiki/Fran%C3%A7ois_Rabelais" title="François Rabelais">François Rabelais</a> in five parts throughout the mid-16th century. It was an early influence of the novel, using an informal style and subversive humor.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESutherland200875–81_56-3"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESutherland200875–81-56">[56]</a></sup>
</p><p>The 17th century in France is known as the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Louis_XIV_of_France" title="Louis XIV of France">Grand Siècle</a> (Great Century). The most famous French authors, beside playwrights, include <a href="/wiki/Jean_de_La_Fontaine" title="Jean de La Fontaine">Jean de La Fontaine</a> and <a href="/wiki/Charles_Perrault" title="Charles Perrault">Charles Perrault</a> known primarily for their <a href="/wiki/Fable" title="Fable">fables</a>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (August 2022)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Italian_literature">Italian literature</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_literature&amp;action=edit&amp;section=34" title="Edit section: Italian literature">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Italian_literature" title="Italian literature">Italian literature</a></div><p>The <a href="/wiki/Italian_Renaissance" title="Italian Renaissance">Italian Renaissance</a> was the starting point of what would become the European Renaissance over the following centuries.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:2_121-1"><a href="#cite_note-:2-121">[121]</a></sup>
</p><p>The earliest work considered an <a href="/wiki/Opera" title="Opera">opera</a> in the sense the work is usually understood dates from around 1597. It is <i><a href="/wiki/Dafne" title="Dafne">Dafne</a></i>, (now lost) written by <a href="/wiki/Jacopo_Peri" title="Jacopo Peri">Jacopo Peri</a> for an elite circle of literate <a href="/wiki/Florence" title="Florence">Florentine</a> <a href="/wiki/Humanism" title="Humanism">humanists</a> who gathered as the "<a href="/wiki/Florentine_Camerata" title="Florentine Camerata">Camerata</a>".<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (August 2022)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Spanish_literature">Spanish literature</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_literature&amp;action=edit&amp;section=35" title="Edit section: Spanish literature">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Spanish_literature" title="Spanish literature">Spanish literature</a></div>
<p><i><a href="/wiki/Don_Quixote" title="Don Quixote">Don Quixote</a></i> by <a href="/wiki/Miguel_de_Cervantes" title="Miguel de Cervantes">Miguel de Cervantes</a> was published in the early-17th century and is recognized as an early novel. It was a <a href="/wiki/Picaresque_novel" title="Picaresque novel">picaresque novel</a> that parodied <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Romance_(heroic_literature)" title="Romance (heroic literature)">chivalric romance</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESutherland200875–81_56-4"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESutherland200875–81-56">[56]</a></sup> The 16th and 17th centuries are recognized as the <a href="/wiki/Spanish_Golden_Age" title="Spanish Golden Age">Spanish Golden Age</a>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_modern_Islamic_world">Early modern Islamic world</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_literature&amp;action=edit&amp;section=36" title="Edit section: Early modern Islamic world">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Islamic_literature" title="Islamic literature">Islamic literature</a></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_modern_East_Asia">Early modern East Asia</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_literature&amp;action=edit&amp;section=37" title="Edit section: Early modern East Asia">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Qing_dynasty">Qing dynasty</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_literature&amp;action=edit&amp;section=38" title="Edit section: Qing dynasty">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Chinese_literature" title="Chinese literature">Chinese literature</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ming_poetry" title="Ming poetry">Ming poetry</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Xyj-sunwukong.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="475" data-file-width="645" decoding="async" height="162" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e0/Xyj-sunwukong.jpg/220px-Xyj-sunwukong.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e0/Xyj-sunwukong.jpg/330px-Xyj-sunwukong.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e0/Xyj-sunwukong.jpg/440px-Xyj-sunwukong.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Xyj-sunwukong.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>An illustrated copy of <i><a href="/wiki/Journey_to_the_West" title="Journey to the West">Journey to the West</a></i></div></div></div>
<p>Printing became prominent in China during the <a href="/wiki/Ming_dynasty" title="Ming dynasty">Ming dynasty</a> in the beginning of the 16th century. By this time, urban areas were highly literate, assisted by the availability of paper in China. Written works were divided into four categories, including classics, histories, philosophy, and <a href="/wiki/Belles-lettres" title="Belles-lettres">belles-lettres</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-129"><a href="#cite_note-129">[129]</a></sup> Among the lower class, <a href="/wiki/Aria" title="Aria">aria</a> and folk songs were common, often performed by entertainers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELuo2011704_130-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELuo2011704-130">[130]</a></sup> Literature was suppressed by the Ming dynasty's founding <a href="/wiki/Hongwu_Emperor" title="Hongwu Emperor">Hongwu Emperor</a>, and writers that did not comply with his rule were executed, beginning a decline in Chinese literature in the 14th and 15th centuries.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELuo2011654–655_131-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELuo2011654–655-131">[131]</a></sup> <i><a href="/wiki/Jiandeng_Xinhua" title="Jiandeng Xinhua">Jiandeng Xinhua</a></i> by <a href="/wiki/Qu_You" title="Qu You">Qu You</a> was a collection of supernatural and <a href="/wiki/Chuanqi_(short_story)" title="Chuanqi (short story)"><i>chuanqi</i></a> stories, and it was one of the few works that maintained influence despite the suppression of literature.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELuo2011719_132-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELuo2011719-132">[132]</a></sup> Variety plays were the most common type of drama in the Early Ming period, and Prince <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Zhu_Youdun&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Zhu Youdun (page does not exist)">Zhu Youdun</a> was the most influential playwright of the time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELuo2011721_133-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELuo2011721-133">[133]</a></sup> A literary revival took place in the Middle Ming period. The revival of poetry was led by literary groups such as the <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Four_Talents_of_Suzhou&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Four Talents of Suzhou (page does not exist)">Four Talents of Suzhou</a> and the <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Earlier_Seven_Masters&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Earlier Seven Masters (page does not exist)">Earlier Seven Masters</a> and continued by groups such as the <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Tang-Song_School&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Tang-Song School (page does not exist)">Tang-Song School</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Latter_Seven_Masters" title="Latter Seven Masters">Latter Seven Masters</a>. <a href="/wiki/Xu_Wei" title="Xu Wei">Xu Wei</a> became an influential poet and prose writer after his death for his use of authenticity and his rejection of the preceding literary schools.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELuo2011667–681_134-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELuo2011667–681-134">[134]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Classic_Chinese_Novels" title="Classic Chinese Novels">Early Chinese novels</a> were developed in the Middle Ming period, including works such as <i><a href="/wiki/Water_Margin" title="Water Margin">Water Margin</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Romance_of_the_Three_Kingdoms" title="Romance of the Three Kingdoms">Romance of the Three Kingdoms</a></i>, and <i><a href="/wiki/Journey_to_the_West" title="Journey to the West">Journey to the West</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELuo2011728_135-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELuo2011728-135">[135]</a></sup>
</p><p>In the Late Ming period, literature increasingly focused on the role of individuals within a society, beginning with the works of <a href="/wiki/Li_Zhi_(philosopher)" title="Li Zhi (philosopher)">Li Zhi</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELuo2011684–685_136-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELuo2011684–685-136">[136]</a></sup> Chinese philosophy developed humanist elements that challenged subservience to community and imperial hierarchy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELuo2011654–655_131-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELuo2011654–655-131">[131]</a></sup> The <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Gong%27an_School&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Gong'an School (page does not exist)">Gong'an School</a>, led by the <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Three_Yuan_Brothers&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Three Yuan Brothers (page does not exist)">Three Yuan Brothers</a>, produced literature of "natural sensibility" that favored delight and simplicity over reason and ambition.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELuo2011687_137-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELuo2011687-137">[137]</a></sup> The <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Jingling_School&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Jingling School (page does not exist)">Jingling School</a>, led by <a href="/wiki/Zhong_Xing" title="Zhong Xing">Zhong Xing</a> and <a href="/wiki/Tan_Yuanchun" title="Tan Yuanchun">Tan Yuanchun</a>, was a response to the Gong'an School that accepted the concept of "natural sensibility" but rejected the carefree style of Gong'an poetry.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELuo2011697–698_138-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELuo2011697–698-138">[138]</a></sup> Novels became more common in the Late Ming period as book publishers emerged.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELuo2011743_139-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELuo2011743-139">[139]</a></sup> <i><a href="/wiki/Jin_Ping_Mei" title="Jin Ping Mei">Jin Ping Mei</a></i> is recognized as a great novel of the Late Ming period and the first novel to follow day-to-day life of commoners.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELuo2011733_140-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELuo2011733-140">[140]</a></sup> <i><a href="/wiki/Huaben" title="Huaben">Huaben</a></i> stories were also common in this period, such as those collected by <a href="/wiki/Feng_Menglong" title="Feng Menglong">Feng Menglong</a> in the <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Three_Words_(collection)&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Three Words (collection) (page does not exist)"><i>Three Words</i></a> and by <a href="/wiki/Ling_Mengchu" title="Ling Mengchu">Ling Mengchu</a> in <i><a href="/wiki/Slapping_the_Table_in_Amazement" title="Slapping the Table in Amazement">Slapping the Table in Amazement</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELuo2011745–750_141-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELuo2011745–750-141">[141]</a></sup> Dramas became more common in the Late Ming period as it became fashionable for the aristocracy to entertain guests with private theater troupes. <a href="/wiki/Tang_Xianzu" title="Tang Xianzu">Tang Xianzu</a> was an influential playright of this period, and the <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Wujiang_School&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Wujiang School (page does not exist)">Wujiang School</a> led by <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Shen_Jing&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Shen Jing (page does not exist)">Shen Jing</a> was responsible for expanding upon drama as an artform with emphasis on its musical elements.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELuo2011753–754_142-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELuo2011753–754-142">[142]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Japanese_literature">Japanese literature</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_literature&amp;action=edit&amp;section=39" title="Edit section: Japanese literature">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Japanese_literature" title="Japanese literature">Japanese literature</a></div>
<p>Wood block printing became popular in the 17th century, and over a thousand books were sold annually by 1670. By the 17th century, increasing social divisions separated drama, with <i><a href="/wiki/Samurai" title="Samurai">samurai</a></i> watching <i>noh</i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Ch%C5%8Dnin" title="Chōnin">chōnin</a></i> watching <i><a href="/wiki/Kabuki" title="Kabuki">kabuki</a></i>. Writers typically dedicated themselves to the government, to a specific clan, to the commons, or to a reclusive lifestyle. <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Ishikawa_J%C5%8Dzan&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Ishikawa Jōzan (page does not exist)">Ishikawa Jōzan</a> was an influential 17th century poet.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKato1997128–129_143-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKato1997128–129-143">[143]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Korean_literature">Korean literature</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_literature&amp;action=edit&amp;section=40" title="Edit section: Korean literature">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Korean_literature" title="Korean literature">Korean literature</a></div>
<p>Korean literature diverged from Chinese literature in the <a href="/wiki/Joseon" title="Joseon">Joseon</a> dynasty. The <i><a href="/wiki/Akjang" title="Akjang">akjang</a></i>, the <i><a href="/wiki/Sijo" title="Sijo">sijo</a></i>, and the <a href="/wiki/Gasa_(poetry)" title="Gasa (poetry)"><i>gasa</i></a> are forms of lyric composition that were popular in the Joseon dynasty.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELee2003148,_168,_189_144-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELee2003148,_168,_189-144">[144]</a></sup> <i><a href="/wiki/Yongbieocheonga" title="Yongbieocheonga">Yongbieocheonga</a></i> was a major collection of poems produced in the 1440s.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELee2003151_145-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELee2003151-145">[145]</a></sup> Korean vernacular fiction was developed by <a href="/wiki/Heo_Gyun" title="Heo Gyun">Heo Gyun</a> at the beginning of the 17th century with <i><a href="/wiki/Hong_Gildong_jeon" title="Hong Gildong jeon">Hong Gildong jeon</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELee2003273_146-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELee2003273-146">[146]</a></sup> The structure of <i>Hong Gildong jeon</i> was used to develop heroic fiction. <i><a href="/wiki/The_Cloud_Dream_of_the_Nine" title="The Cloud Dream of the Nine">The Cloud Dream of the Nine</a></i> was written by <a href="/wiki/Kim_Man-jung" title="Kim Man-jung">Kim Man-jung</a> to reflect historical and philosophical changes of the time using dream structure.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELee2003276–277_147-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELee2003276–277-147">[147]</a></sup> <i><a href="/wiki/Pansori" title="Pansori">Pansori</a></i> fiction was developed in the early 18th century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELee2003284_148-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELee2003284-148">[148]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_modern_Southeast_Asia">Early modern Southeast Asia</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_literature&amp;action=edit&amp;section=41" title="Edit section: Early modern Southeast Asia">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Cambodian_literature" title="Cambodian literature">Cambodian literature</a>, <a href="/wiki/Vietnamese_literature" title="Vietnamese literature">Vietnamese literature</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Thai_literature" title="Thai literature">Thai literature</a></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_modern_India">Early modern India</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_literature&amp;action=edit&amp;section=42" title="Edit section: Early modern India">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Indian_literature" title="Indian literature">Indian literature</a></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Pre-colonial_Africa">Pre-colonial Africa</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_literature&amp;action=edit&amp;section=43" title="Edit section: Pre-colonial Africa">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/African_literature" title="African literature">African literature</a></div>
<h2><span id="Late_modern_.2818th_century.E2.80.9320th_century.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Late_modern_(18th_century–20th_century)">Late modern (18th century–20th century)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_literature&amp;action=edit&amp;section=44" title="Edit section: Late modern (18th century–20th century)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/18th_century_literature" title="18th century literature">18th century literature</a>, <a href="/wiki/19th_century_in_literature" title="19th century in literature">19th century in literature</a>, and <a href="/wiki/20th_century_in_literature" title="20th century in literature">20th century in literature</a></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Late_modern_Europe">Late modern Europe</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_literature&amp;action=edit&amp;section=45" title="Edit section: Late modern Europe">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The 18th century was <a href="/wiki/Age_of_Enlightenment" title="Age of Enlightenment">Age of Enlightenment</a> and its most important authors are <a href="/wiki/Voltaire" title="Voltaire">Voltaire</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jean-Jacques_Rousseau" title="Jean-Jacques Rousseau">Jean-Jacques Rousseau</a>, <a href="/wiki/Immanuel_Kant" title="Immanuel Kant">Immanuel Kant</a> and <a href="/wiki/Adam_Smith" title="Adam Smith">Adam Smith</a>. The second half of the century sees the beginnings of <a href="/wiki/Romanticism" title="Romanticism">Romanticism</a> with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Goethe" title="Goethe">Goethe</a>. In the later 19th century, Romanticism is countered by <a href="/wiki/Literary_realism" title="Literary realism">Realism</a> and <a href="/wiki/Naturalism_(literature)" title="Naturalism (literature)">Naturalism</a>. The late 19th century, known as the <i><a href="/wiki/Belle_%C3%89poque" title="Belle Époque">Belle Époque</a></i>, with its <i><a href="/wiki/Fin_de_si%C3%A8cle" title="Fin de siècle">Fin de siècle</a></i> retrospectively appeared as a "golden age" of European culture, cut short by the outbreak of <a href="/wiki/World_War_I" title="World War I">World War I</a> in 1914. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Modernist_literature" title="Modernist literature">Modernist literature</a> was written from roughly 1900 to 1940.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (August 2022)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p>In Britain, the 19th century was dominated by the <a href="/wiki/Victorian_era" title="Victorian era">Victorian era</a>, characterized by Romanticism, with <a href="/wiki/Romantic_poetry" title="Romantic poetry">Romantic poets</a> such as <a href="/wiki/William_Wordsworth" title="William Wordsworth">William Wordsworth</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lord_Byron" title="Lord Byron">Lord Byron</a> or <a href="/wiki/Samuel_Taylor_Coleridge" title="Samuel Taylor Coleridge">Samuel Taylor Coleridge</a> and genres such as the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gothic_novel" title="Gothic novel">gothic novel</a>. <a href="/wiki/Charles_Dickens" title="Charles Dickens">Charles Dickens</a>, perhaps the most famous novelist in the history of English literature, was active during this time and contributed to the novel's emergence as the leading literary genre of Victorian England.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (August 2022)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p>In Denmark, the early 19th century <a href="/wiki/Danish_Golden_Age" title="Danish Golden Age">Golden Age</a> produced prolific literary authors such as <a href="/wiki/S%C3%B8ren_Kierkegaard" title="Søren Kierkegaard">Søren Kierkegaard</a> and <a href="/wiki/Hans_Christian_Andersen" title="Hans Christian Andersen">Hans Christian Andersen</a>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (August 2022)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p>In Germany, the <i><a href="/wiki/Sturm_und_Drang" title="Sturm und Drang">Sturm und Drang</a></i> period of the late 18th century merges into a <a href="/wiki/Classicism" title="Classicism">Classicist</a> and <a href="/wiki/Romanticism" title="Romanticism">Romantic</a> period, epitomized by the long era of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Goethe" title="Goethe">Goethe</a>'s activity, covering the first third of the century. The conservative <i><a href="/wiki/Biedermeier" title="Biedermeier">Biedermeier</a></i> style conflicts with the radical <i><a href="/wiki/Vorm%C3%A4rz" title="Vormärz">Vormärz</a></i> in the turbulent period separating the end of the Napoleonic wars from the <a href="/wiki/Revolutions_of_1848" title="Revolutions of 1848">Revolutions of 1848</a>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (August 2022)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="United_States">United States</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_literature&amp;action=edit&amp;section=46" title="Edit section: United States">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/American_literature" title="American literature">American literature</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Huckleberry_Finn_book.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1000" data-file-width="918" decoding="async" height="240" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/61/Huckleberry_Finn_book.JPG/220px-Huckleberry_Finn_book.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/61/Huckleberry_Finn_book.JPG/330px-Huckleberry_Finn_book.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/61/Huckleberry_Finn_book.JPG/440px-Huckleberry_Finn_book.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Huckleberry_Finn_book.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>1884 cover of <i><a href="/wiki/Adventures_of_Huckleberry_Finn" title="Adventures of Huckleberry Finn">Adventures of Huckleberry Finn</a></i></div></div></div>
<p>Literature of the <a href="/wiki/American_Revolution" title="American Revolution">American Revolution</a> included <i><a href="/wiki/A_Summary_View_of_the_Rights_of_British_America" title="A Summary View of the Rights of British America">A Summary View of the Rights of British America</a></i> by <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Jefferson" title="Thomas Jefferson">Thomas Jefferson</a> and <i><a href="/wiki/Common_Sense" title="Common Sense">Common Sense</a></i> by <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Paine" title="Thomas Paine">Thomas Paine</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGray201128_149-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGray201128-149">[149]</a></sup> <i><a href="/wiki/The_Power_of_Sympathy" title="The Power of Sympathy">The Power of Sympathy</a></i> by <a href="/wiki/William_Hill_Brown" title="William Hill Brown">William Hill Brown</a> was the first American novel, written in a realistic style with a clear moral message.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGray201141_150-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGray201141-150">[150]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Judith_Sargent_Murray" title="Judith Sargent Murray">Judith Sargent Murray</a> is credited with establishing American feminism through her essays and poetry.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGray201132_151-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGray201132-151">[151]</a></sup> The United States quickly achieved widespread literacy in the early-19th century, and many authors made fiction-writing their primary source of income. <a href="/wiki/Washington_Irving" title="Washington Irving">Washington Irving</a> set precedent for comic literature and short stories, and he established the Knickerbocker School that wrote affectionately of New York. <a href="/wiki/James_Fenimore_Cooper" title="James Fenimore Cooper">James Fenimore Cooper</a> similarly set the precedent for American <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sea_stories" title="Sea stories">sea stories</a>, novels of manners, political satire, dynastic novels, and <a href="/wiki/American_frontier" title="American frontier">frontier</a> stories.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGray201147–49_152-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGray201147–49-152">[152]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Edgar_Allan_Poe" title="Edgar Allan Poe">Edgar Allan Poe</a> was highly influential in fiction writing, poetry, and essays, and he is particularly credited for his contributions to <a href="/wiki/Gothic_fiction" title="Gothic fiction">Gothic fiction</a> and <a href="/wiki/Mystery_fiction" title="Mystery fiction">mystery fiction</a> as genres.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGray201154–57_153-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGray201154–57-153">[153]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Nathaniel_Hawthorne" title="Nathaniel Hawthorne">Nathaniel Hawthorne</a> wrote moralistic stories such as <i><a href="/wiki/The_Scarlet_Letter" title="The Scarlet Letter">The Scarlet Letter</a></i> inspired by colonial-era <a href="/wiki/Puritans" title="Puritans">Puritan</a> literature. <a href="/wiki/Herman_Melville" title="Herman Melville">Herman Melville</a> explored human contradiction in sea stories such as <i><a href="/wiki/Moby-Dick" title="Moby-Dick">Moby-Dick</a></i>, though his work did not become influential until the 1920s.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGray201193–101_154-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGray201193–101-154">[154]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/William_Cullen_Bryant" title="William Cullen Bryant">William Cullen Bryant</a> was credited as the "founding father of American poetry", <a href="/wiki/Walt_Whitman" title="Walt Whitman">Walt Whitman</a> is celebrated as defining the essence of America in his day with his poetry collection <i><a href="/wiki/Leaves_of_Grass" title="Leaves of Grass">Leaves of Grass</a></i>. <a href="/wiki/Emily_Dickinson" title="Emily Dickinson">Emily Dickinson</a> was refused to publish her poetry during her lifetime, but her works of this period were later acclaimed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGray2011106–114_155-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGray2011106–114-155">[155]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Transcendentalism" title="Transcendentalism">Transcendentalism</a> evolved as a philosophical school in the early-19th century, and authors such as <a href="/wiki/Ralph_Waldo_Emerson" title="Ralph Waldo Emerson">Ralph Waldo Emerson</a> contributed to transcendentalist literature.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGray201159_156-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGray201159-156">[156]</a></sup> Many works were written on the topic of slavery in the early United States, including <i><a href="/wiki/Uncle_Tom%27s_Cabin" title="Uncle Tom's Cabin">Uncle Tom's Cabin</a></i> by <a href="/wiki/Harriet_Beecher_Stowe" title="Harriet Beecher Stowe">Harriet Beecher Stowe</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGray201181_157-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGray201181-157">[157]</a></sup> Freed slaves such as <a href="/wiki/Frederick_Douglass" title="Frederick Douglass">Frederick Douglass</a> and <a href="/wiki/Harriet_Jacobs" title="Harriet Jacobs">Harriet Jacobs</a> also wrote autobiographies that promoted the cause of <a href="/wiki/Abolitionism_in_the_United_States" title="Abolitionism in the United States">abolitionism</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGray201166–69_158-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGray201166–69-158">[158]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Native_American_literature" title="Native American literature">Native American literature</a> in the early-19th century was in conflict between retaining traditional elements and incorporating elements of contemporary American literature.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGray201171–72_159-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGray201171–72-159">[159]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Literary_realism" title="Literary realism">Realism</a> and <a href="/wiki/Naturalism_(literature)" title="Naturalism (literature)">naturalism</a> became popular in the United States in the late-19th century. <a href="/wiki/Mark_Twain" title="Mark Twain">Mark Twain</a> saw wide acclaim for his novels, including <i><a href="/wiki/Adventures_of_Huckleberry_Finn" title="Adventures of Huckleberry Finn">Adventures of Huckleberry Finn</a></i>. Regionalism developed in which different literary traditions evolved in the <a href="/wiki/Western_United_States" title="Western United States">West</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Midwestern_United_States" title="Midwestern United States">Midwest</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Southern_United_States" title="Southern United States">South</a>, and <a href="/wiki/New_England" title="New England">New England</a>. <a href="/wiki/African_Americans" title="African Americans">African American</a> and <a href="/wiki/Native_Americans_in_the_United_States" title="Native Americans in the United States">Native American</a> literature also evolved as distinct literary traditions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGray2011115–130_160-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGray2011115–130-160">[160]</a></sup>
</p><p>A movement of backlash against small-town and middle-class values emerged at the beginning of the 20th century, including authors such as <a href="/wiki/Theodore_Dreiser" title="Theodore Dreiser">Theodore Dreiser</a>, <a href="/wiki/H._L._Mencken" title="H. L. Mencken">H. L. Mencken</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sinclair_Lewis" title="Sinclair Lewis">Sinclair Lewis</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Sherwood_Anderson" title="Sherwood Anderson">Sherwood Anderson</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGray2011167–169_161-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGray2011167–169-161">[161]</a></sup> Civil rights literature of the early-20th century was written by authors such as <a href="/wiki/W._E._B._Du_Bois" title="W. E. B. Du Bois">W. E. B. Du Bois</a>, <a href="/wiki/Booker_T._Washington" title="Booker T. Washington">Booker T. Washington</a>, and <a href="/wiki/James_Weldon_Johnson" title="James Weldon Johnson">James Weldon Johnson</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGray2011159_162-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGray2011159-162">[162]</a></sup> American poetry expanded significantly during the 20th century, introducing or incorporating <a href="/wiki/Modernist_poetry" title="Modernist poetry">modernist poetry</a> traditions such as <a href="/wiki/Imagism" title="Imagism">Imagism</a>, <a href="/wiki/Vorticism" title="Vorticism">Vorticism</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Objectivism_(poetry)" title="Objectivism (poetry)">Objectivism</a>. Poets such as <a href="/wiki/Edwin_Arlington_Robinson" title="Edwin Arlington Robinson">Edwin Arlington Robinson</a>, <a href="/wiki/Robert_Frost" title="Robert Frost">Robert Frost</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Robinson_Jeffers" title="Robinson Jeffers">Robinson Jeffers</a> were influential in developing poetic form.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGray2011171_163-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGray2011171-163">[163]</a></sup> Authors such as <a href="/wiki/F._Scott_Fitzgerald" title="F. Scott Fitzgerald">F.
