Scott Fitzgerald</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ernest_Hemingway" title="Ernest Hemingway">Ernest Hemingway</a>, and <a href="/wiki/William_Faulkner" title="William Faulkner">William Faulkner</a> analyzed their own places in American society through modernist novels,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGray2011196–206_164-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGray2011196–206-164">[164]</a></sup> while authors such as <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Wolfe" title="Thomas Wolfe">Thomas Wolfe</a> and <a href="/wiki/John_Steinbeck" title="John Steinbeck">John Steinbeck</a> sought to find a future identity for the United States.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGray2011223–226_165-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGray2011223–226-165">[165]</a></sup> Counter-movements emerged in response to modernism, including traditionalism in the South and populism in <a href="/wiki/Chicago_literature" title="Chicago literature">Chicago literature</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGray2011211–218_166-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGray2011211–218-166">[166]</a></sup> The early-20th century also saw the emergence of <a href="/wiki/Theater_in_the_United_States" title="Theater in the United States">American drama</a> distinct from that of Europe.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGray2011206–207_167-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGray2011206–207-167">[167]</a></sup> Popular genre fiction at the time included <a href="/wiki/Western_(genre)" title="Western (genre)">westerns</a> and <a href="/wiki/Detective_fiction" title="Detective fiction">detective fiction</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGray2011242_168-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGray2011242-168">[168]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Canada">Canada</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_literature&amp;action=edit&amp;section=47" title="Edit section: Canada">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Canadian_literature" title="Canadian literature">Canadian literature</a></div>
<p>Early Canadian novels adapted the literary trends of Europe. <i><a href="/wiki/The_History_of_Emily_Montague" title="The History of Emily Montague">The History of Emily Montague</a></i> was the first Canadian novel, published in 1769. The aftermath of the <a href="/wiki/Seven_Years%27_War" title="Seven Years' War">Seven Years' War</a> was a significant influence on Canadian literature in the 18th and 19th centuries as <a href="/wiki/Canada_(New_France)" title="Canada (New France)">French Canada</a> was transferred to the British.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELane201138_169-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELane201138-169">[169]</a></sup> Popular novels, such as those of <a href="/wiki/Julia_Catherine_Beckwith" title="Julia Catherine Beckwith">Julia Catherine Beckwith</a>, came about in the early-19th century with an emphasis on day-to-day experiences rather than grand narrative.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELane201157–61_170-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELane201157–61-170">[170]</a></sup> Nature featured prominently in Canadian literature by the mid-19th century. <a href="/wiki/Edward_Hartley_Dewart" title="Edward Hartley Dewart">Edward Hartley Dewart</a> was a prominent figure in early Canadian poetry, having published the first Canadian poetry anthology in 1864. He identified the formation of a Canadian literary body as a part of a conflict between colonial origins and a distinct Canadian identity. <a href="/wiki/Octave_Cr%C3%A9mazie" title="Octave Crémazie">Octave Crémazie</a> led the French Canadian literature movement in the 1860s. Notable Canadian poets of this period included <a href="/wiki/Charles_Sangster" title="Charles Sangster">Charles Sangster</a>, <a href="/wiki/Alexander_McLachlan_(poet)" title="Alexander McLachlan (poet)">Alexander McLachlan</a>, <a href="/wiki/Charles_Heavysege" title="Charles Heavysege">Charles Heavysege</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Confederation_Poets" title="Confederation Poets">Confederation Poets</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELane201121–26_171-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELane201121–26-171">[171]</a></sup> Victorian poetry remained prominent in Canada through the early-20th century, though international modernist movements such as Imagism gained influence after <a href="/wiki/World_War_I" title="World War I">World War I</a>. Poets such as <a href="/wiki/Arthur_Stringer_(writer)" title="Arthur Stringer (writer)">Arthur Stringer</a> and <a href="/wiki/Frank_Oliver_Call" title="Frank Oliver Call">Frank Oliver Call</a> lead a <a href="/wiki/Free_verse" title="Free verse">free verse</a> movement in the 1910s and 1920s.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELane201194–96_172-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELane201194–96-172">[172]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Latin_America">Latin America</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_literature&amp;action=edit&amp;section=48" title="Edit section: Latin America">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Latin_American_literature" title="Latin American literature">Latin American literature</a></div><p>Latin American literature diverged from European literature and began as its own literary tradition at the start of the 19th century. The primary change was in the subject matter and theme of the literature, while the form remained relatively European. It began with an individualist trend associated with the <a class="mw-redirect mw-disambig" href="/wiki/Latin_American_Independence" title="Latin American Independence">Latin American independence</a> movements that were developing at the time. Within this movement developed the individual literature of each Latin American country as they formed their own national identities. <a href="/wiki/Andr%C3%A9s_Bello" title="Andrés Bello">Andrés Bello</a> is recognized as a founder of modern literature in <a href="/wiki/Hispanic_America" title="Hispanic America">Hispanic America</a>, and he is credited for popularizing Romanticism in the region. The balance of national and continental literary identities was a major issue in early Latin American literature, particularly within the Spanish language.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-173"><a href="#cite_note-173">[173]</a></sup>
</p><h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Late_modern_Islamic_world">Late modern Islamic world</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_literature&amp;action=edit&amp;section=49" title="Edit section: Late modern Islamic world">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Islamic_literature" title="Islamic literature">Islamic literature</a></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Late_modern_East_Asia">Late modern East Asia</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_literature&amp;action=edit&amp;section=50" title="Edit section: Late modern East Asia">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Qing_dynasty_and_Republic_of_China">Qing dynasty and Republic of China</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_literature&amp;action=edit&amp;section=51" title="Edit section: Qing dynasty and Republic of China">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Chinese_literature" title="Chinese literature">Chinese literature</a> and <a href="/wiki/Qing_poetry" title="Qing poetry">Qing poetry</a></div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Qing_dynasty" title="Qing dynasty">Qing dynasty</a> saw the return of prose literature as a vehicle primarily for Confucian teaching, and individualist literature declined.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELuo2011787–788_174-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELuo2011787–788-174">[174]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Three_Masters_of_Jiangdong" title="Three Masters of Jiangdong">Three Masters of Jiangdong</a> were the most prominent poets during the <a href="/wiki/Transition_from_Ming_to_Qing" title="Transition from Ming to Qing">transition from Ming to Qing</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELuo2011773_175-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELuo2011773-175">[175]</a></sup> <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Wang_Shizhen_(poet)&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Wang Shizhen (poet) (page does not exist)">Wang Shizhen</a> led poetry trends in the Early Qing period through the expression of sentimentality through imagery and prosody rather than vocabulary, particularly with his <i><a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Autumn_Willows:_Four_Poems&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Autumn Willows: Four Poems (page does not exist)">Autumn Willows: Four Poems</a></i>. Song lyrics also reemerged as a popular poetic form as literary critics viewed the individual aria as unrefined.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELuo2011778–781_176-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELuo2011778–781-176">[176]</a></sup> Heroic fiction was adapted to popular novels in the Early Qing period. <a href="/wiki/Supernatural_fiction" title="Supernatural fiction">Supernatural fiction</a> also became popular, including <i><a href="/wiki/Strange_Tales_from_a_Chinese_Studio" title="Strange Tales from a Chinese Studio">Strange Tales from a Chinese Studio</a></i> by <a href="/wiki/Pu_Songling" title="Pu Songling">Pu Songling</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELuo2011845–848_177-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELuo2011845–848-177">[177]</a></sup>
</p><p>In the Middle Qing period, <a href="/wiki/Shen_Deqian" title="Shen Deqian">Shen Deqian</a>, <a href="/wiki/Weng_Fanggang" title="Weng Fanggang">Weng Fanggang</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Yuan_Mei" title="Yuan Mei">Yuan Mei</a> each formed new trends in poetry in opposition to the style of Wang Shizhen.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELuo2011793_178-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELuo2011793-178">[178]</a></sup> <i><a href="/wiki/Pianwen" title="Pianwen">Pianwen</a></i> became popular among prose writers in the Middle Qing period in opposition to the <a href="/wiki/Tongcheng_school" title="Tongcheng school">Tongcheng school</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELuo2011805_179-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELuo2011805-179">[179]</a></sup> <i><a href="/wiki/Six_Records_of_a_Floating_Life" title="Six Records of a Floating Life">Six Records of a Floating Life</a></i> was an autobiography by <a href="/wiki/Shen_Fu" title="Shen Fu">Shen Fu</a> that emphasized direct language and emotional appeal over style.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELuo2011810–811_180-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELuo2011810–811-180">[180]</a></sup> Performing arts of the Middle Qing period included the northern <i><a href="/wiki/Guci" title="Guci">guci</a></i> drum lyric and the southern <i><a href="/wiki/Tanci" title="Tanci">tanci</a></i> strummed lyric, the latter of which was often written by women poets.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELuo2011819_181-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELuo2011819-181">[181]</a></sup> Novels of the Middle Qing period included <a href="/wiki/The_Scholars_(novel)" title="The Scholars (novel)"><i>The Scholars</i></a> and <i><a href="/wiki/Dream_of_the_Red_Chamber" title="Dream of the Red Chamber">Dream of the Red Chamber</a></i>, both of which eschewed popular taste in favor of philosophy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELuo2011861_182-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELuo2011861-182">[182]</a></sup> Drama went out of fashion among the <a href="/wiki/Qing_literati" title="Qing literati">Qing literati</a>, though theatrical performances remained common entertainment for the public.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELuo2011839_183-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELuo2011839-183">[183]</a></sup>  In the Late Qing period, Western culture was a significant influence on Chiense society.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELuo2011823_184-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELuo2011823-184">[184]</a></sup> The <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Tongguang_school&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Tongguang school (page does not exist)">Tongguang school</a> was popular among adherents to typical Chinese poetry, styled after that of the Song dynasty.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELuo2011827_185-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELuo2011827-185">[185]</a></sup> Chivalrous novels telling the tales of folk heroes, political novels that were critical of the falling Qing government, and genre fiction all became popular in the final years of the Qing dynasty.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELuo2011882–886_186-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELuo2011882–886-186">[186]</a></sup>
</p><p>Literature in the <a href="/wiki/Republic_of_China_(1912%E2%80%931949)" title="Republic of China (1912–1949)">Republic of China</a> reflected the societal changes brought about by the <a href="/wiki/1911_Revolution" title="1911 Revolution">1911 Revolution</a>. Chinese literature broke away from classical tradition and literary restrictions with the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/New_Literature_Movement" title="New Literature Movement">New Literature Movement</a> in 1919, beginning with works such as <i>Experiments</i> by <a href="/wiki/Hu_Shih" title="Hu Shih">Hu Shih</a>, <a href="/wiki/Diary_of_a_Madman_(Lu_Xun)" title="Diary of a Madman (Lu Xun)"><i>Diary of a Madman</i></a> by <a href="/wiki/Lu_Xun" title="Lu Xun">Lu Xun</a>, and <i>The Goddess</i> by <a href="/wiki/Guo_Moruo" title="Guo Moruo">Guo Moruo</a>. The <a href="/wiki/May_Fourth_Movement" title="May Fourth Movement">May Fourth Movement</a> was a further cultural movement that promoted the use of <a href="/wiki/Written_vernacular_Chinese" title="Written vernacular Chinese">vernacular Chinese</a>. Advocates of <a href="/wiki/Liberalism_in_China" title="Liberalism in China">liberal reform in China</a> supported these shifts in literary tradition to challenge imperial institutions, and these works typically supported humanism and individualism. A refutation of individualism emerged in response at the onset of the <a href="/wiki/Chinese_Civil_War" title="Chinese Civil War">Chinese Civil War</a>, and revolutionary literature was spread in support of the <a href="/wiki/Chinese_Red_Army" title="Chinese Red Army">Chinese Red Army</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-187"><a href="#cite_note-187">[187]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Japanese_literature_2">Japanese literature</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_literature&amp;action=edit&amp;section=52" title="Edit section: Japanese literature">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Japanese_literature" title="Japanese literature">Japanese literature</a></div>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Korean_literature_2">Korean literature</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_literature&amp;action=edit&amp;section=53" title="Edit section: Korean literature">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Korean_literature" title="Korean literature">Korean literature</a></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Late_modern_Southeast_Asia">Late modern Southeast Asia</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_literature&amp;action=edit&amp;section=54" title="Edit section: Late modern Southeast Asia">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Cambodian_literature" title="Cambodian literature">Cambodian literature</a>, <a href="/wiki/Vietnamese_literature" title="Vietnamese literature">Vietnamese literature</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Thai_literature" title="Thai literature">Thai literature</a></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Late_modern_India">Late modern India</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_literature&amp;action=edit&amp;section=55" title="Edit section: Late modern India">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Indian_literature" title="Indian literature">Indian literature</a></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Colonial_Africa">Colonial Africa</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_literature&amp;action=edit&amp;section=56" title="Edit section: Colonial Africa">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/African_literature" title="African literature">African literature</a></div>
<h2><span id="Contemporary_.2820th_century.E2.80.9321st_century.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Contemporary_(20th_century–21st_century)">Contemporary (20th century–21st century)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_literature&amp;action=edit&amp;section=57" title="Edit section: Contemporary (20th century–21st century)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Contemporary_literature" title="Contemporary literature">Contemporary literature</a></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/21st_century_in_literature" title="21st century in literature">21st century in literature</a></div>
<p>Contemporary literature is defined as literature written after the end of <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">World War II</a> in 1945. <a href="/wiki/Postmodern_literature" title="Postmodern literature">Postmodern literature</a> was written from roughly 1945 to 1980. Popular literature developed its own genres such as <a href="/wiki/Fantasy_literature" title="Fantasy literature">fantasy</a> and <a href="/wiki/Science_fiction" title="Science fiction">science fiction</a>. Ignored by mainstream <a href="/wiki/Literary_criticism" title="Literary criticism">literary criticism</a>, these genres develop their own establishments and critical awards, such as the <i><a href="/wiki/Nebula_Award" title="Nebula Award">Nebula Award</a></i> (since 1965), the <i><a href="/wiki/British_Fantasy_Award" title="British Fantasy Award">British Fantasy Award</a></i> (since 1971) or the <i><a href="/wiki/Mythopoeic_Awards" title="Mythopoeic Awards">Mythopoeic Awards</a></i> (since 1971).<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (August 2022)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p>

<li id="cite_note-Woolmer-35"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-Woolmer_35-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation journal cs1" id="CITEREFMark_Woolmer">Mark Woolmer (ed.). <a class="external text" href="https://www.academia.edu/30935044" rel="nofollow">"Phoenician: A Companion to Ancient Phoenicia"</a>. <i>A Companion to Ancient Phoenicia, ed. Mark Woolmer</i>: 4. <q>Altogether, the known Phoenician texts number nearly seven thousand. The majority of these were collected in three volumes constituting the first part of the Corpus Inscriptionum Semiticarum (CIS), begun in 1867 under the editorial direction of the famous French scholar Ernest Renan (1823–1892), continued by J.-B. Chabot and concluded in 1962 by James G. Février. The CIS corpus includes 176 "Phoenician" inscriptions and 5982 "Punic" inscriptions (see below on these labels).</q></cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal&amp;rft.genre=article&amp;rft.jtitle=A+Companion+to+Ancient+Phoenicia%2C+ed.+Mark+Woolmer&amp;rft.atitle=Phoenician%3A+A+Companion+to+Ancient+Phoenicia&amp;rft.pages=4&amp;rft_id=https%3A%2F%2Fwww.academia.edu%2F30935044&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AHistory+of+literature"></span></span>
</li>
